Napoleon
========

From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia

Emperor of the French (r. 1804–1814, 1815)

**Napoleon Bonaparte**[[b]](#cite_note-2) (born **Napoleone Buonaparte**;[[1]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2008axv-3)[[c]](#cite_note-4) 15 August 1769 – 5 May 1821), later known by his [regnal name](/wiki/Regnal_name "Regnal name") **Napoleon I**, was a French general and statesman who rose to prominence during the [French Revolution](/wiki/French_Revolution "French Revolution") and led [a series of military campaigns](/wiki/Military_career_of_Napoleon_Bonaparte "Military career of Napoleon Bonaparte") across Europe during the [French Revolutionary and Napoleonic Wars](/wiki/French_Revolutionary_and_Napoleonic_Wars "French Revolutionary and Napoleonic Wars") from 1796 to 1815. He led the [French Republic](/wiki/French_First_Republic "French First Republic") as [First Consul](/wiki/French_Consulate "French Consulate") from 1799 to 1804, then ruled the [French Empire](/wiki/First_French_Empire "First French Empire") as [Emperor of the French](/wiki/Emperor_of_the_French "Emperor of the French") from 1804 to 1814, and briefly again in 1815. He was [King](/wiki/King_of_Italy "King of Italy") of [Italy](/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy_(Napoleonic) "Kingdom of Italy (Napoleonic)") from 1805 to 1814 and [Protector](/wiki/Protector_of_the_Confederation_of_the_Rhine "Protector of the Confederation of the Rhine") of the [Confederation of the Rhine](/wiki/Confederation_of_the_Rhine "Confederation of the Rhine") from 1806 to 1813.

Born on the island of [Corsica](/wiki/Corsica "Corsica") to a family of Italian origin, Napoleon moved to mainland France in 1779 and was commissioned as an officer in the [French Royal Army](/wiki/French_Royal_Army "French Royal Army") in 1785. He supported the French Revolution in 1789, and promoted its cause in Corsica. He rose rapidly through the ranks after winning the [siege of Toulon](/wiki/Siege_of_Toulon_(1793) "Siege of Toulon (1793)") in 1793 and defeating royalist insurgents in Paris on [13 Vendémiaire](/wiki/13_Vend%C3%A9miaire "13 Vendémiaire") in 1795. In 1796, Napoleon commanded [a military campaign](/wiki/Italian_Campaign_of_1796%E2%80%931797 "Italian Campaign of 1796–1797") against the [Austrians](/wiki/Habsburg_monarchy "Habsburg monarchy") and their Italian allies in the [War of the First Coalition](/wiki/War_of_the_First_Coalition "War of the First Coalition"), scoring decisive victories and becoming a national hero. He led an [invasion of Egypt and Syria](/wiki/French_invasion_of_Egypt_and_Syria "French invasion of Egypt and Syria") in 1798 which served as a springboard to political power. In November 1799, Napoleon engineered the [Coup of 18 Brumaire](/wiki/Coup_of_18_Brumaire "Coup of 18 Brumaire") against the [Directory](/wiki/French_Directory "French Directory"), and became First Consul of the Republic. He won the [Battle of Marengo](/wiki/Battle_of_Marengo "Battle of Marengo") in 1800, which secured France's victory in the [War of the Second Coalition](/wiki/War_of_the_Second_Coalition "War of the Second Coalition"), and in 1803 [sold the territory of Louisiana](/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase "Louisiana Purchase") to the [United States](/wiki/United_States "United States"). In December 1804, Napoleon [crowned himself](/wiki/Coronation_of_Napoleon "Coronation of Napoleon") Emperor of the French, further expanding his power.

The breakdown of the [Treaty of Amiens](/wiki/Treaty_of_Amiens "Treaty of Amiens") led to the [War of the Third Coalition](/wiki/War_of_the_Third_Coalition "War of the Third Coalition") by 1805. Napoleon shattered the coalition with a decisive victory at the [Battle of Austerlitz](/wiki/Battle_of_Austerlitz "Battle of Austerlitz"), which led to the [dissolution of the Holy Roman Empire](/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Holy_Roman_Empire "Dissolution of the Holy Roman Empire"). In the [War of the Fourth Coalition](/wiki/War_of_the_Fourth_Coalition "War of the Fourth Coalition"), Napoleon defeated [Prussia](/wiki/Kingdom_of_Prussia "Kingdom of Prussia") at the [Battle of Jena–Auerstedt](/wiki/Battle_of_Jena%E2%80%93Auerstedt "Battle of Jena–Auerstedt") in 1806, marched his *[Grande Armée](/wiki/Grande_Arm%C3%A9e "Grande Armée")* into Eastern Europe, and defeated the [Russians](/wiki/Russian_Empire "Russian Empire") in 1807 at the [Battle of Friedland](/wiki/Battle_of_Friedland "Battle of Friedland"). Seeking to extend his [trade embargo against Britain](/wiki/Continental_System "Continental System"), Napoleon invaded the [Iberian Peninsula](/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula "Iberian Peninsula") and installed his brother [Joseph](/wiki/Joseph_Bonaparte "Joseph Bonaparte") as [King of Spain](/wiki/King_of_Spain "King of Spain") in 1808, provoking the [Peninsular War](/wiki/Peninsular_War "Peninsular War"). In 1809, the Austrians again challenged France in the [War of the Fifth Coalition](/wiki/War_of_the_Fifth_Coalition "War of the Fifth Coalition"), in which Napoleon solidified his grip over Europe after winning the [Battle of Wagram](/wiki/Battle_of_Wagram "Battle of Wagram"). In summer 1812, he launched [an invasion of Russia](/wiki/French_invasion_of_Russia "French invasion of Russia"), briefly [occupying Moscow](/wiki/French_occupation_of_Moscow "French occupation of Moscow") before conducting a catastrophic retreat of his army that winter. In 1813, Prussia and Austria joined Russia in the [War of the Sixth Coalition](/wiki/War_of_the_Sixth_Coalition "War of the Sixth Coalition"), in which Napoleon was decisively defeated at the [Battle of Leipzig](/wiki/Battle_of_Leipzig "Battle of Leipzig"). The coalition [invaded France](/wiki/1814_campaign_in_France "1814 campaign in France") and captured Paris, [forcing Napoleon to abdicate](/wiki/Abdication_of_Napoleon,_1814 "Abdication of Napoleon, 1814") in April 1814. They exiled him to the Mediterranean island of [Elba](/wiki/Elba "Elba") and restored the [Bourbons to power](/wiki/Bourbon_Restoration_in_France "Bourbon Restoration in France"). Ten months later, Napoleon escaped from Elba on a brig, landed in France with a thousand men, and marched on Paris, again taking control of the country. His opponents responded by forming a [Seventh Coalition](/wiki/Seventh_Coalition "Seventh Coalition"), which defeated him at the [Battle of Waterloo](/wiki/Battle_of_Waterloo "Battle of Waterloo") in June 1815. Napoleon was exiled to the remote island of [Saint Helena](/wiki/Saint_Helena "Saint Helena") in the South Atlantic, where he died of stomach cancer in 1821, aged 51.

Napoleon is considered one of the greatest military commanders in history and [Napoleonic tactics](/wiki/Napoleonic_tactics "Napoleonic tactics") are still studied at military schools worldwide. His [legacy](/wiki/Legacy_of_Napoleon "Legacy of Napoleon") endures through the modernizing legal and administrative reforms he enacted in France and Western Europe, embodied in the [Napoleonic Code](/wiki/Napoleonic_Code "Napoleonic Code"). He established a system of public education,[[2]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrab200356-5) abolished the vestiges of [feudalism](/wiki/Feudalism "Feudalism"),[[3]](#cite_note-Broers-6) [emancipated Jews](/wiki/Napoleon_and_the_Jews "Napoleon and the Jews") and other religious minorities,[[4]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200438–40-7) abolished the [Spanish Inquisition](/wiki/Spanish_Inquisition "Spanish Inquisition"),[[5]](#cite_note-8) enacted the principle of [equality before the law](/wiki/Equality_before_the_law "Equality before the law") for an emerging middle class,[[6]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFremont-BarnesFisher2004336-9) and centralized state power at the expense of religious authorities.[[7]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrab2017204–211-10) His conquests acted as a catalyst for political change and the development of [nation states](/wiki/Nation_state "Nation state"). However, he is controversial due to his role in wars which devastated Europe, his [looting](/wiki/Napoleonic_looting_of_art "Napoleonic looting of art") of conquered territories, and his mixed record on civil rights. He abolished the free press, ended directly elected representative government, exiled and jailed critics of his regime, reinstated slavery in France's colonies except for [Haiti](/wiki/Haitian_Revolution "Haitian Revolution"), banned the entry of blacks and [mulattos](/wiki/Mulatto "Mulatto") into France, reduced the civil rights of women and children in France, reintroduced a hereditary monarchy and nobility,[[8]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2015a574–76,_582–84-11)[[9]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200432–34,_50–51-12)[[10]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell201552-13) and violently repressed popular uprisings against his rule.[[11]](#cite_note-Repa222-14)

Early life
----------

Napoleon's family was of Italian origin. His paternal ancestors, the Buonapartes, descended from a minor [Tuscan](/wiki/Tuscany "Tuscany") noble family who emigrated to Corsica in the 16th century and his maternal ancestors, the Ramolinos, descended from a noble family from [Lombardy](/wiki/Lombardy_(historical_region) "Lombardy (historical region)").[[12]](#cite_note-15)

Napoleon's parents, [Carlo Maria Buonaparte](/wiki/Carlo_Maria_Buonaparte "Carlo Maria Buonaparte") and [Maria Letizia Ramolino](/wiki/Maria_Letizia_Ramolino "Maria Letizia Ramolino"), lived in the [Maison Bonaparte](/wiki/Maison_Bonaparte "Maison Bonaparte") home in [Ajaccio](/wiki/Ajaccio "Ajaccio"), where Napoleon was born on 15 August 1769. He had an elder brother, [Joseph](/wiki/Joseph_Bonaparte "Joseph Bonaparte"), and, later, six younger siblings: [Lucien](/wiki/Lucien_Bonaparte "Lucien Bonaparte"), [Elisa](/wiki/Elisa_Bonaparte "Elisa Bonaparte"), [Louis](/wiki/Louis_Bonaparte "Louis Bonaparte"), [Pauline](/wiki/Pauline_Bonaparte "Pauline Bonaparte"), [Caroline](/wiki/Caroline_Bonaparte "Caroline Bonaparte"), and [Jérôme](/wiki/J%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_Bonaparte "Jérôme Bonaparte").[[13]](#cite_note-16) Five more siblings were stillborn or did not survive infancy.[[14]](#cite_note-17) Napoleon was baptized as a [Catholic](/wiki/Catholic_Church "Catholic Church"), under the name *Napoleone di Buonaparte*. In his youth, his name was also spelled as *Nabulione*, *Nabulio*, *Napolionne*, and *Napulione*.[[15]](#cite_note-dwyerxv2-18)

Napoleon was born one year after the [Republic of Genoa](/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa "Republic of Genoa") ceded Corsica to France.[[16]](#cite_note-McLynn-1997b-19)[[d]](#cite_note-23) His father supported [Pasquale Paoli](/wiki/Pasquale_Paoli "Pasquale Paoli") during the Corsican war of independence against France. After the Corsican defeat at the [Battle of Ponte Novu](/wiki/Battle_of_Ponte_Novu "Battle of Ponte Novu") in 1769 and Paoli's exile in Britain, Carlo became friends with the French governor [Charles Louis de Marbeuf](/wiki/Charles_Louis_de_Marbeuf "Charles Louis de Marbeuf"), who became his patron and godfather to Napoleon.[[20]](#cite_note-24)[[21]](#cite_note-Ellis22-25) With Mabeuf's support, Carlo was named Corsican representative to the court of [Louis XVI](/wiki/Louis_XVI "Louis XVI") and Napoleon obtained a royal bursary to a military academy in France.[[22]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECronin199420–21-26)[[23]](#cite_note-27)

The dominant influence of Napoleon's childhood was his mother, whose firm discipline restrained a rambunctious child.[[22]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECronin199420–21-26) Later in life, Napoleon said, "The future destiny of the child is always the work of the mother."[[24]](#cite_note-28) Napoleon's noble, moderately affluent background afforded him greater opportunities to study than were available to a typical Corsican of the time.[[25]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECronin199427-29)

In January 1779, at age 9, Napoleon moved to the French mainland and enrolled at a religious school in [Autun](/wiki/Autun "Autun") to improve his French (his mother tongue was the Corsican dialect of Italian).[[26]](#cite_note-Parker-1971-30)[[27]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts201411-31)[[28]](#cite_note-32) Although he eventually became fluent in French, he spoke with a Corsican accent and his French spelling was poor.[[29]](#cite_note-33)

In May, he transferred to the military academy at [Brienne-le-Château](/wiki/Brienne-le-Ch%C3%A2teau "Brienne-le-Château") where he was routinely bullied by his peers for his accent, birthplace, short stature, mannerisms, and poor French.[[26]](#cite_note-Parker-1971-30) He became reserved and melancholic, applying himself to reading. An examiner observed that Napoleon "has always been distinguished for his application in mathematics. He is fairly well acquainted with history and geography ... This boy would make an excellent sailor".[[e]](#cite_note-35)[[31]](#cite_note-36)

One story of Napoleon at the school is that he led junior students to victory against senior students in a snowball fight, which allegedly showed his leadership abilities.[[32]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChandler197312–14-37) But the story was only told after Napoleon had become famous.[[33]](#cite_note-38) In his later years at Brienne, Napoleon became an outspoken Corsican nationalist and admirer of Paoli.[[34]](#cite_note-39)

In September 1784, Napoleon was admitted to the *[École militaire](/wiki/%C3%89cole_militaire "École militaire")* in Paris where he trained to become an artillery officer. He excelled at mathematics, and read widely in geography, history and literature. However, he was poor at French and German.[[35]](#cite_note-40) His father's death in February 1785 cut the family income and forced him to complete the two-year course in one year. In September he was examined by the famed scientist [Pierre-Simon Laplace](/wiki/Pierre-Simon_Laplace "Pierre-Simon Laplace") and became the first Corsican to graduate from the *École militaire*.[[36]](#cite_note-dwyer422-41)[[37]](#cite_note-42)

Early career
------------

### Return to Corsica

Upon graduating in September 1785, Bonaparte was commissioned a [second lieutenant](/wiki/Second_lieutenant "Second lieutenant") in [*La Fère* artillery regiment](/wiki/1st_Artillery_Regiment_(France) "1st Artillery Regiment (France)").[[38]](#cite_note-rxviii2-43) He served in [Valence](/wiki/Valence_(city) "Valence (city)") and [Auxonne](/wiki/Auxonne "Auxonne") until after the outbreak of the French Revolution in 1789, but spent long periods of leave in Corsica which fed his Corsican nationalism.[[39]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts2014Chapter_1,_pp._3–28-44)[[40]](#cite_note-45) In September 1789, he returned to Corsica and promoted the French revolutionary cause. Paoli returned to the island in July 1790, but he had no sympathy for Bonaparte, as he deemed his father a traitor for having deserted the cause of Corsican independence.[[41]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts2014Chapter_2,_pp._29–53-46)[[42]](#cite_note-47)

Bonaparte plunged into a complex three-way struggle among royalists, revolutionaries, and Corsican nationalists. He became a supporter of the [Jacobins](/wiki/Jacobins "Jacobins") and joined the pro-French Corsican Republicans who opposed Paoli's policy and his aspirations to secede.[[43]](#cite_note-48) He was given command over a battalion of Corsican volunteers and promoted to captain in the regular army in 1792, despite exceeding his leave of absence and a dispute between his volunteers and the French garrison in Ajaccio.[[44]](#cite_note-49)[[45]](#cite_note-50)

In February 1793, Bonaparte took part in the failed [French expedition to Sardinia](/wiki/French_expedition_to_Sardinia "French expedition to Sardinia"). Following allegations that Paoli had sabotaged the expedition and that his regime was corrupt and incompetent, the French [National Convention](/wiki/National_Convention "National Convention") outlawed him. In early June, Bonaparte and 400 French troops failed to capture Ajaccio from Corsican volunteers and the island was now controlled by Paoli's supporters. When Bonaparte learned that the Corsican assembly had condemned him and his family, the Buonapartes fled to Toulon on the French mainland.[[46]](#cite_note-51)[[47]](#cite_note-52)

### Siege of Toulon

Main article: [Siege of Toulon (1793)](/wiki/Siege_of_Toulon_(1793) "Siege of Toulon (1793)")

Bonaparte returned to his regiment in Nice and was made captain of a coastal battery.[[48]](#cite_note-53) In July 1793, he published a pamphlet, *[Le souper de Beaucaire](/wiki/Le_souper_de_Beaucaire "Le souper de Beaucaire")* (Supper at [Beaucaire](/wiki/Beaucaire,_Gard "Beaucaire, Gard")), demonstrating his support for the [National Convention](/wiki/National_Convention "National Convention") which was now heavily influenced by the Jacobins.[[49]](#cite_note-54)[[50]](#cite_note-55)

In September, with the help of his fellow Corsican [Antoine Christophe Saliceti](/wiki/Antoine_Christophe_Saliceti "Antoine Christophe Saliceti"), Bonaparte was appointed artillery commander of the republican forces sent to recapture the port of Toulon which was occupied by Allied forces.[[51]](#cite_note-56) He quickly increased the available artillery and proposed a plan to capture a hill fort where republican guns could dominate the city's harbour and force the Allies to evacuate. The successful assault on the position on 16–17 December led to the capture of the city.[[52]](#cite_note-57)

Toulon brought Bonaparte to the attention of powerful men including [Augustin Robespierre](/wiki/Augustin_Robespierre "Augustin Robespierre"), the younger brother of [Maximilien Robespierre](/wiki/Maximilien_Robespierre "Maximilien Robespierre"), a leading Jacobin. He was promoted to brigadier general and put in charge of defences on the Mediterranean coast. In February 1794, he was made artillery commander of the Army of Italy and devised plans to attack the Kingdom of Sardinia.[[53]](#cite_note-58)[[54]](#cite_note-59)

The French army carried out Bonaparte's plan in the [Second Battle of Saorgio](/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Saorgio "Second Battle of Saorgio") in April 1794, and then advanced to seize [Ormea](/wiki/Ormea "Ormea") in the mountains. From Ormea, it headed west to outflank the Austro-Sardinian positions around [Saorge](/wiki/Saorge "Saorge"). After this campaign, Augustin Robespierre sent Bonaparte on a mission to the Republic of Genoa to determine the country's intentions towards France.[[55]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGueniffey2015137–159-60)[[56]](#cite_note-61)

### 13 Vendémiaire

Main article: [13 Vendémiaire](/wiki/13_Vend%C3%A9miaire "13 Vendémiaire")

After the [Fall of Maximilien Robespierre](/wiki/Fall_of_Maximilien_Robespierre "Fall of Maximilien Robespierre") in July 1794, Bonaparte's association with leading Jacobins made him politically suspect to the new regime. He was arrested on 9 August but released two weeks later.[[57]](#cite_note-62)[[58]](#cite_note-63)[[59]](#cite_note-64) He was asked to draw up plans to attack Italian positions as part of France's war with Austria and, in March 1795, he took part in an expedition to take back Corsica from the British, but the French were repulsed by the Royal Navy.[[60]](#cite_note-65)

From 1794, Bonaparte was in a romantic relationship with [Désirée Clary](/wiki/D%C3%A9sir%C3%A9e_Clary "Désirée Clary") whose sister [Julie Clary](/wiki/Julie_Clary "Julie Clary") had married Bonaparte's elder brother Joseph.[[61]](#cite_note-66)[[62]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2008a159–63-67) In April 1795, Bonaparte was assigned to the [Army of the West](/wiki/Army_of_the_West_(1793) "Army of the West (1793)"), which was engaged in the [War in the Vendée](/wiki/War_in_the_Vend%C3%A9e "War in the Vendée")—a civil war and royalist counter-revolution in the [Vendée](/wiki/Vend%C3%A9e "Vendée") region. As an infantry command, it was a demotion from artillery general and he pleaded poor health to avoid the posting.[[63]](#cite_note-68) During this period, he wrote the romantic novella *[Clisson et Eugénie](/wiki/Clisson_et_Eug%C3%A9nie "Clisson et Eugénie")*, about a soldier and his lover, in a clear parallel to Bonaparte's own relationship with Clary.[[64]](#cite_note-Dwyer_2008a-69)

In August, he obtained a position with the Bureau of [Topography](/wiki/Topography "Topography") where he worked on military planning.[[64]](#cite_note-Dwyer_2008a-69) On 15 September, Bonaparte was removed from the list of generals in regular service for refusing to serve in the Vendée campaign.[[65]](#cite_note-70) He sought a transfer to [Constantinople](/wiki/Constantinople "Constantinople") to offer his services to Sultan [Selim III](/wiki/Selim_III "Selim III"). The request was eventually granted, but he never took up the post.[[66]](#cite_note-71)[[67]](#cite_note-72)

On 3 October, royalists in Paris declared a rebellion against the National Convention.[[68]](#cite_note-m962-73) [Paul Barras](/wiki/Paul_Barras "Paul Barras"), a leader of the [Thermidorian Reaction](/wiki/Thermidorian_Reaction "Thermidorian Reaction"), knew of Bonaparte's military exploits at Toulon and made him second in command of the forces defending the convention in the [Tuileries Palace](/wiki/Tuileries_Palace "Tuileries Palace"). Bonaparte had seen the massacre of the King's Swiss Guard during the [Insurrection of 10 August 1792](/wiki/Insurrection_of_10_August_1792 "Insurrection of 10 August 1792") there three years earlier and realized that artillery would be the key to its defence. He ordered a young cavalry officer, [Joachim Murat](/wiki/Joachim_Murat "Joachim Murat"), to seize cannons and Bonaparte deployed them in key positions. On 5 October 1795—*13 Vendémiaire An IV* in the [French Republican calendar](/wiki/French_Republican_calendar "French Republican calendar")—he fired on the rebels with canister rounds (later called: "a whiff of [grapeshot](/wiki/Grapeshot "Grapeshot")"). About 300 to 1,400 rebels died in the uprising.[[68]](#cite_note-m962-73)[[69]](#cite_note-74)[[70]](#cite_note-75)

Bonaparte's role in defeating the rebellion earned him and his family the patronage of the new government, the [French Directory](/wiki/French_Directory "French Directory").[[71]](#cite_note-76) On 26 October, he was promoted to commander of the Army of the Interior, and in January 1796 he was appointed head of the Army of Italy.[[72]](#cite_note-77)

Within weeks of the *Vendémiaire* uprising*,* Bonaparte was romantically involved with [Joséphine de Beauharnais](/wiki/Jos%C3%A9phine_de_Beauharnais "Joséphine de Beauharnais"), the former mistress of Barras. Josephine had been born in the French colonies in the [Lesser Antilles](/wiki/Lesser_Antilles "Lesser Antilles"), and her family owned slaves on [sugar plantations](/wiki/Sugar_plantations_in_the_Caribbean "Sugar plantations in the Caribbean")[[73]](#cite_note-:3-78) The couple married on 9 March 1796 in a civil ceremony.[[74]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEnglund201092–94-79) Bonaparte now habitually styled himself "Napoleon Bonaparte" rather than using the Italian form "Napoleone di Buonaparte."[[75]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChandler19663-80)[[76]](#cite_note-81)[[77]](#cite_note-82)

### First Italian campaign

Main article: [Italian Campaign of 1796-1797](/wiki/Italian_Campaign_of_1796-1797 "Italian Campaign of 1796-1797")

Two days after the marriage, Bonaparte left Paris to take command of the Army of Italy. He went on the offensive, hoping to defeat the Kingdom of Sardinia in [Piedmont](/wiki/Piedmont "Piedmont") before their Austrian allies could intervene. In a series of victories during the [Montenotte campaign](/wiki/Montenotte_campaign "Montenotte campaign"), he knocked the Piedmontese out of the war in two weeks.[[78]](#cite_note-83) The French then focused on the Austrians, laying siege to [Mantua](/wiki/Siege_of_Mantua_(1796%E2%80%931797) "Siege of Mantua (1796–1797)"). The Austrians launched offensives against the French to break the siege, but Bonaparte defeated every relief effort, winning the [Battle of Castiglione](/wiki/Battle_of_Castiglione "Battle of Castiglione"), the [Battle of Bassano](/wiki/Battle_of_Bassano "Battle of Bassano"), the [Battle of Arcole](/wiki/Battle_of_Arcole "Battle of Arcole"), and the [Battle of Rivoli](/wiki/Battle_of_Rivoli "Battle of Rivoli"). The French triumph at Rivoli in January 1797 led to the collapse of the Austrian position in Italy. At Rivoli, Austria lost 43% of its soldiers dead, wounded or taken prisoner.[[79]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell201529-84)[[80]](#cite_note-85)

The French then invaded the heartlands of the [House of Habsburg](/wiki/House_of_Habsburg "House of Habsburg"). French forces in Southern Germany had been defeated by [Archduke Charles, Duke of Teschen](/wiki/Archduke_Charles,_Duke_of_Teschen "Archduke Charles, Duke of Teschen") in 1796, but Charles withdrew his forces to protect Vienna after learning of Bonaparte's assault. In their first encounter, Bonaparte pushed Charles back and advanced deep into Austrian territory after winning the [Battle of Tarvis](/wiki/Battle_of_Tarvis_(1797) "Battle of Tarvis (1797)") in March 1797. Alarmed by the French thrust that reached [Leoben](/wiki/Leoben "Leoben"), about 100 km from Vienna, the Austrians sued for peace.[[81]](#cite_note-86)[[82]](#cite_note-87)

The preliminary [peace of Leoben](/wiki/Peace_of_Leoben "Peace of Leoben"), signed on 18 April, gave France control of most of northern Italy and the [Low Countries](/wiki/Low_Countries "Low Countries"), and promised to partition the [Republic of Venice](/wiki/Republic_of_Venice "Republic of Venice") with Austria.[[83]](#cite_note-88) Bonaparte marched on Venice and [forced its surrender](/wiki/Fall_of_the_Republic_of_Venice#12_May_1797:_the_Fall_of_the_Venetian_Republic "Fall of the Republic of Venice"), ending 1,100 years of Venetian independence. He authorized the French to loot treasures such as the [Horses of Saint Mark](/wiki/Horses_of_Saint_Mark "Horses of Saint Mark").[[84]](#cite_note-89)[[85]](#cite_note-90)

In this Italian campaign, Bonaparte's army captured 150,000 prisoners, 540 cannons, and 170 [standards](/wiki/Flag "Flag"). The French army fought 67 actions and won 18 [pitched battles](/wiki/Pitched_battle "Pitched battle") through superior artillery technology and Bonaparte's tactics.[[86]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLynn1997135-91) Bonaparte extracted an estimated 45 million French pounds from Italy during the campaign, another 12 million pounds in precious metals and jewels, and more than 300 paintings and sculptures.[[87]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell201530-92)

During the campaign, Bonaparte became increasingly influential in French politics. He founded two newspapers: one for the troops in his army and one for circulation in France.[[88]](#cite_note-93) The royalists attacked him for looting Italy and warned that he might become a dictator.[[89]](#cite_note-94)

Bonaparte sent General [Charles-Pierre Augereau](/wiki/Charles-Pierre_Augereau "Charles-Pierre Augereau") to Paris to support a *coup d'état* that purged royalists from the legislative councils on 4 September—the [Coup of 18 Fructidor](/wiki/Coup_of_18_Fructidor "Coup of 18 Fructidor"). This left Barras and his republican allies in control again but more dependent upon Bonaparte who finalized peace terms with Austria by the [Treaty of Campo Formio](/wiki/Treaty_of_Campo_Formio "Treaty of Campo Formio").[[90]](#cite_note-95) Bonaparte returned to Paris on 5 December 1797 as a hero.[[91]](#cite_note-96) He met [Charles Maurice de Talleyrand](/wiki/Charles_Maurice_de_Talleyrand "Charles Maurice de Talleyrand"), France's Foreign Minister, and took command of the Army of England for the planned invasion of Britain.[[92]](#cite_note-97)

### Egyptian expedition

Main article: [French campaign in Egypt and Syria](/wiki/French_campaign_in_Egypt_and_Syria "French campaign in Egypt and Syria")

After two months of planning, Bonaparte decided that France's naval strength was not yet sufficient to confront the [British Royal Navy.](/wiki/Royal_Navy "Royal Navy") He decided on a military expedition to seize [Egypt](/wiki/Egypt_Eyalet "Egypt Eyalet") and thereby undermine Britain's access to its [trade interests in India](/wiki/Company_rule_in_India#Trade "Company rule in India").[[38]](#cite_note-rxviii2-43) Bonaparte wished to establish a French presence in the Middle East and join forces with [Tipu Sultan](/wiki/Tipu_Sultan "Tipu Sultan"), the [Sultan of Mysore](/wiki/Kingdom_of_Mysore "Kingdom of Mysore"), an enemy of the British.[[93]](#cite_note-Watson-98) Bonaparte assured the Directory that "as soon as he had conquered Egypt, he will establish relations with the Indian princes and, together with them, attack the English in their possessions".[[94]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmini200012-99) The Directory agreed in order to secure a trade route to the [Indian subcontinent](/wiki/Indian_subcontinent "Indian subcontinent").[[95]](#cite_note-100)

In May 1798, Bonaparte was elected a member of the [French Academy of Sciences](/wiki/French_Academy_of_Sciences "French Academy of Sciences"). His Egyptian expedition included a group of 167 scientists, with mathematicians, naturalists, chemists, and [geodesists](/wiki/Geodesy "Geodesy") among them. Their discoveries included the [Rosetta Stone](/wiki/Rosetta_Stone "Rosetta Stone"), and their work was published in the *[Description de l'Égypte](/wiki/Description_de_l%27%C3%89gypte "Description de l'Égypte")* in 1809.[[96]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEnglund2010127–28-101) En route to Egypt, Bonaparte reached [Hospitaller Malta](/wiki/Hospitaller_Malta "Hospitaller Malta") on 9 June 1798, then controlled by the [Knights Hospitaller](/wiki/Knights_Hospitaller "Knights Hospitaller"). Grand Master [Ferdinand von Hompesch zu Bolheim](/wiki/Ferdinand_von_Hompesch_zu_Bolheim "Ferdinand von Hompesch zu Bolheim") surrendered after token resistance, and Bonaparte captured an important naval base with the loss of only three men.[[97]](#cite_note-102)

Bonaparte and his expedition eluded pursuit by the Royal Navy and landed at [Alexandria](/wiki/Alexandria "Alexandria") on 1 July.[[38]](#cite_note-rxviii2-43) He fought the [Battle of Shubra Khit](/wiki/Battle_of_Shubra_Khit "Battle of Shubra Khit") against the [Mamluks](/wiki/Mamluk "Mamluk"), Egypt's ruling military caste. This helped the French practise their defensive tactic for the [Battle of the Pyramids](/wiki/Battle_of_the_Pyramids "Battle of the Pyramids") on 21 July, about 24 km (15 mi) from the [pyramids](/wiki/Egyptian_pyramids "Egyptian pyramids"). Bonaparte's forces of 25,000 roughly equalled those of the Mamluks' Egyptian cavalry. Twenty-nine French[[98]](#cite_note-103) and approximately 2,000 Egyptians were killed. The victory boosted the French army's morale.[[99]](#cite_note-104)

On 1 August 1798, the British fleet under [Sir Horatio Nelson](/wiki/Horatio_Nelson,_1st_Viscount_Nelson "Horatio Nelson, 1st Viscount Nelson") captured or destroyed all but two vessels of the French fleet in the [Battle of the Nile](/wiki/Battle_of_the_Nile "Battle of the Nile"), preventing Bonaparte from strengthening the French position in the Mediterranean.[[100]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamoyski2018188-105) His army had succeeded in a temporary increase of French power in Egypt, though it faced repeated uprisings.[[101]](#cite_note-106) In early 1799, he moved an army into the [Ottoman province](/wiki/Wilayah "Wilayah") of Damascus (Syria and [Galilee](/wiki/Galilee "Galilee")). Bonaparte led these 13,000 French soldiers in the conquest of the coastal towns of [Arish](/wiki/Arish "Arish"), [Gaza](/wiki/Gaza_City "Gaza City"), [Jaffa](/wiki/Jaffa "Jaffa"), and [Haifa](/wiki/Haifa "Haifa").[[102]](#cite_note-107) The [attack on Jaffa](/wiki/Siege_of_Jaffa "Siege of Jaffa") was particularly brutal. Bonaparte discovered that many of the defenders were former prisoners of war, ostensibly on parole, so he ordered the garrison and some 1,500–5,000 prisoners to be executed by bayonet or drowning.[[103]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamoyski2018198-108)[[104]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell201539–40-109)[[105]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLynn1997280-110) Men, women, and children were robbed and murdered for three days.[[106]](#cite_note-111)

Bonaparte began with an army of 13,000 men. 1,500 were reported missing, 1,200 died in combat, and thousands perished from disease—mostly [bubonic plague](/wiki/Bubonic_plague "Bubonic plague"). He failed to [reduce the fortress](/wiki/Siege_of_Acre_(1799) "Siege of Acre (1799)") of [Acre](/wiki/Acre,_Israel "Acre, Israel"), so he marched his army back to Egypt in May. Bonaparte was alleged to have ordered plague-stricken men to be poisoned with opium to speed the retreat.[[107]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGueniffey2015500–502-112) Back in Egypt on 25 July, Bonaparte defeated an [Ottoman amphibious invasion at Abukir](/wiki/Battle_of_Abukir_(1799) "Battle of Abukir (1799)").[[108]](#cite_note-113)

Bonaparte stayed informed of European affairs. He learned that France had suffered a [series of defeats](/wiki/Campaigns_of_1799_in_the_French_Revolutionary_Wars "Campaigns of 1799 in the French Revolutionary Wars") in the [War of the Second Coalition](/wiki/War_of_the_Second_Coalition "War of the Second Coalition").[[109]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConnelly200657-114) On 24 August 1799, fearing that the Republic's future was in doubt, he took advantage of the temporary departure of British ships from French coastal ports and set sail for France, despite the fact that he had received no explicit orders from Paris.[[110]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamoyski2018205–206-115) The army was left in the charge of [Jean-Baptiste Kléber](/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Kl%C3%A9ber "Jean-Baptiste Kléber").[[111]](#cite_note-116)

Ruler of France
---------------

Main article: [Napoleonic era](/wiki/Napoleonic_era "Napoleonic era")

### *18 Brumaire*

Main article: [18 Brumaire](/wiki/Coup_of_18_Brumaire "Coup of 18 Brumaire")

Unknown to Bonaparte, the Directory had sent him orders to return from Egypt with his army to ward off a possible invasion of France, but these messages never arrived.[[109]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConnelly200657-114) By the time that he reached Paris in October, France's situation had been improved by a series of victories. The Republic, however, was bankrupt and the ineffective Directory was unpopular.[[112]](#cite_note-117) Despite the failures in Egypt, Bonaparte returned to a hero's welcome. The Directory discussed Bonaparte's desertion but was too weak to punish him.[[109]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConnelly200657-114)

Bonaparte formed an alliance with Talleyrand and leading members of the [Council of Five Hundred](/wiki/Council_of_Five_Hundred "Council of Five Hundred") and Directory: Lucien Bonaparte, [Emmanuel Joseph Sieyès](/wiki/Emmanuel_Joseph_Siey%C3%A8s "Emmanuel Joseph Sieyès"), [Roger Ducos](/wiki/Roger_Ducos "Roger Ducos") and [Joseph Fouché](/wiki/Joseph_Fouch%C3%A9 "Joseph Fouché") to overthrow the government. On 9 November 1799 (*18 Brumaire* according to the revolutionary calendar), the conspirators launched a coup, and the following day, backed by grenadiers with fixed bayonets, forced the Council of Five Hundred to dissolve the Directory and appoint Bonaparte, Sieyès and Ducos provisional consuls.[[113]](#cite_note-118)[[114]](#cite_note-119)

### French Consulate

Main articles: [French Consulate](/wiki/French_Consulate "French Consulate") and [War of the Second Coalition](/wiki/War_of_the_Second_Coalition "War of the Second Coalition")

On 15 December, Bonaparte introduced the [Constitution of the Year VIII](/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Year_VIII "Constitution of the Year VIII"), under which three consuls were appointed for 10 years. Real power lay with Bonaparte as First Consul, and his preferred candidates [Cambacérès](/wiki/Jean-Jacques-R%C3%A9gis_de_Cambac%C3%A9r%C3%A8s "Jean-Jacques-Régis de Cambacérès") and [Charles-François Lebrun](/wiki/Charles-Fran%C3%A7ois_Lebrun "Charles-François Lebrun") were appointed as second and third consuls who only had an advisory role. The constitution also established a [Legislative Body](/wiki/Corps_l%C3%A9gislatif "Corps législatif") and [Tribunate](/wiki/Tribunat "Tribunat") which were selected from indirectly elected candidates, and a [Senate](/wiki/S%C3%A9nat_conservateur "Sénat conservateur") and [Council of State](/wiki/Conseil_d%27%C3%89tat "Conseil d'État") which were effectively nominated by the executive.[[115]](#cite_note-120)

The new constitution was approved by plebiscite on 7 February 1800. The official count was over three million in favour and 1,562 against. Lucien, however, had doubled the count of the "yes" vote to give the false impression that a majority of those eligible to vote had approved the constitution.[[116]](#cite_note-121)[[117]](#cite_note-Lyons,_Martyn_1994,_p._1112-122)

Historians have variously described Bonaparte's new regime as "dictatorship by plebiscite",[[117]](#cite_note-Lyons,_Martyn_1994,_p._1112-122) "absolutist rule decked out in the spirit of the age",[[118]](#cite_note-123) and "soft despotism".[[119]](#cite_note-124) Local and regional administration was reformed to concentrate power in the central government,[[120]](#cite_note-125) censorship was introduced, and most opposition newspapers were closed down to stifle dissent.[[121]](#cite_note-126) Royalist and regional revolts were dealt with by a combination of amnesties for those who lay down their arms and brutal repression of those who continued to resist.[[122]](#cite_note-127)[[123]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2015a256-128)[[124]](#cite_note-129) Bonaparte also improved state finances by securing loans under a promise to defend private property, raising taxes on tobacco, alcohol and salt, and extracting levies from France's satellite republics.[[125]](#cite_note-130)

Bonaparte believed that the best way to secure his regime was by a victorious peace.[[126]](#cite_note-131) In May 1800, he led an army across the Swiss Alps into Italy, aiming to surprise the Austrian armies that had reoccupied the peninsula when Bonaparte was still in Egypt. After a difficult crossing over the Alps,[[f]](#cite_note-133) the French captured Milan on 2 June.[[128]](#cite_note-Chandler-1966a-134)[[129]](#cite_note-Zamoyski-2018-135)

The French confronted an Austrian army under [Michael von Melas](/wiki/Michael_von_Melas "Michael von Melas") at [Marengo](/wiki/Battle_of_Marengo "Battle of Marengo") on 14 June.[[128]](#cite_note-Chandler-1966a-134)[[129]](#cite_note-Zamoyski-2018-135) The Austrians fielded about 30,000 soldiers while Bonaparte commanded 24,000 troops.[[130]](#cite_note-136) The Austrians' initial attack surprised the French who were gradually driven back.[[131]](#cite_note-137) Late in the afternoon, however, a full division under [Desaix](/wiki/Louis_Desaix "Louis Desaix") arrived on the field and reversed the tide of the battle. The Austrian army fled leaving behind 14,000 casualties.[[132]](#cite_note-Chandler,_David_1966,_p._2962-138) The following day, the Austrians signed an armistice and agreed to abandon Northern Italy.[[132]](#cite_note-Chandler,_David_1966,_p._2962-138)

When peace negotiations with Austria stalled, Bonaparte reopened hostilities in November. A French army under [General Moreau](/wiki/Jean_Victor_Moreau "Jean Victor Moreau") swept through Bavaria and scored an overwhelming victory over the Austrians at [Hohenlinden](/wiki/Battle_of_Hohenlinden "Battle of Hohenlinden") in December. The Austrians capitulated and signed the [Treaty of Lunéville](/wiki/Treaty_of_Lun%C3%A9ville "Treaty of Lunéville") in February 1801. The treaty reaffirmed and expanded earlier French gains at Campo Formio.[[133]](#cite_note-Schom_1997,_p.3022-139)

Bonaparte's triumph at Marengo increased his popularity and political authority. However, he still faced royalist plots and feared Jacobin influence, especially in the army. Several assassination plots, including the *[Conspiration des poignards](/wiki/Conspiration_des_poignards "Conspiration des poignards")* (Dagger plot) in October 1800 and the [Plot of the Rue Saint-Nicaise](/wiki/Plot_of_the_rue_Saint-Nicaise "Plot of the rue Saint-Nicaise") two months later, gave him a pretext to arrest about 100 suspected Jacobins and royalists, some of whom were shot and many others deported to penal colonies.[[134]](#cite_note-140)[[135]](#cite_note-141)

#### Temporary peace in Europe

See also: [Haitian Revolution](/wiki/Haitian_Revolution "Haitian Revolution")

After a decade of war, France and Britain signed the [Treaty of Amiens](/wiki/Treaty_of_Amiens "Treaty of Amiens") in March 1802, bringing the Revolutionary Wars to an end. Under the treaty, Britain agreed to withdraw from most of the colonies it had recently captured from France and her allies, and France agreed to evacuate Naples. In April, Bonaparte publicly celebrated the peace and his controversial [Concordat of 1801](/wiki/Concordat_of_1801 "Concordat of 1801") with [Pope Pius VII](/wiki/Pope_Pius_VII "Pope Pius VII") under which the Pope recognized Bonaparte's regime and the regime recognized Catholicism as the majority religion of France. In a further step towards national reconciliation (known as "fusion"), Bonaparte offered an amnesty to most émigrés who wished to return to France.[[136]](#cite_note-142)[[137]](#cite_note-143)

With Europe at peace and the economy recovering, Bonaparte became increasingly popular, both domestically and abroad.[[138]](#cite_note-144) In May 1802, the Council of State recommended a [new plebiscite](/wiki/1802_French_constitutional_referendum "1802 French constitutional referendum") asking the French people to make "Napoleon Bonaparte" Consul for life. (It was the first time his first name was officially used by the regime.)[[139]](#cite_note-145) About 3.6 million voted "yes" and 8,374 "no." 40%-60% of eligible Frenchmen voted, the highest turnout for a plebiscite since the Revolution.[[140]](#cite_note-146)[[141]](#cite_note-147)

France had regained her overseas colonies under Amiens but did not control them all. The French National Convention had voted to abolish slavery in February 1794, but, in May 1802, Bonaparte reintroduced it in all the recovered colonies except [Saint-Domingue](/wiki/Saint-Domingue "Saint-Domingue") and [Guadeloupe](/wiki/Guadeloupe "Guadeloupe") which were under the control of rebel generals. A French military expedition under [Antoine Richepanse](/wiki/Antoine_Richepance "Antoine Richepance") regained control of Guadeloupe and slavery was reintroduced there on 16 July.[[142]](#cite_note-148)

Saint-Domingue was the most profitable of the colonies – a major source of sugar, coffee and indigo – but was under the control of the former slave [Toussaint Louverture](/wiki/Toussaint_Louverture "Toussaint Louverture").[[143]](#cite_note-149) Bonaparte sent the [Saint-Domingue expedition](/wiki/Saint-Domingue_expedition "Saint-Domingue expedition") under his brother-in-law [General Leclerc](/wiki/Charles_Leclerc_(general,_born_1772) "Charles Leclerc (general, born 1772)") to retake the colony and they landed there in February 1802 with 29,000 men. Although Toussaint was captured and sent to France in July, the expedition ultimately failed due to high rates of disease and a string of defeats against rebel commander [Jean-Jacques Dessalines](/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Dessalines "Jean-Jacques Dessalines"). In May 1803, Bonaparte acknowledged defeat, and the last 8,000 French troops left the island. The former slaves proclaimed the independent republic of [Haiti](/wiki/Haiti "Haiti") in 1804.[[144]](#cite_note-150)[[145]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts2014303-151)

As war with Britain again loomed in 1803, Bonaparte realized that his American colony of Louisiana would be difficult to defend.[[146]](#cite_note-152) In need of funds, he agreed to the [Louisiana Purchase](/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase "Louisiana Purchase") with the United States, doubling the latter's size. The price was $15 million.[[147]](#cite_note-Roberts,_Andrew_201422-153)[[148]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConnelly200670-154)[[149]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroers2015389–390-155)

The peace with Britain was uneasy. Britain did not evacuate [Malta](/wiki/Malta "Malta") as promised and protested against Bonaparte's annexation of Piedmont and his [Act of Mediation](/wiki/Act_of_Mediation "Act of Mediation"), which established a new [Swiss Confederation](/wiki/Switzerland_in_the_Napoleonic_era "Switzerland in the Napoleonic era"). Neither of these territories were covered by Amiens, but they inflamed tensions significantly, as did Bonaparte's occupation of Holland and apparent ambitions in India.[[150]](#cite_note-156)[[151]](#cite_note-157) The dispute culminated in a declaration of war by Britain in May 1803. Bonaparte responded by reassembling the invasion camp at Boulogne and ordering the arrest of every British male between eighteen and sixty years old in France and its dependencies as a [prisoner of war](/wiki/Prisoner_of_war "Prisoner of war").[[152]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamoyski2018338–339-158)

### French Empire

Main article: [First French Empire](/wiki/First_French_Empire "First French Empire")

See also: [Coronation of Napoleon I](/wiki/Coronation_of_Napoleon_I "Coronation of Napoleon I") and [Napoleonic Wars](/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars "Napoleonic Wars")

#### Bonaparte becomes Napoleon I

In February 1804, Bonaparte's police made a series of arrests in relation to a royalist plot to kidnap or assassinate him that involved the British government, Moreau and an unnamed [Bourbon](/wiki/House_of_Bourbon "House of Bourbon") prince. On the advice of his foreign minister, Talleyrand, Napoleon ordered the kidnapping of the [Duke of Enghien](/wiki/Louis_Antoine,_Duke_of_Enghien "Louis Antoine, Duke of Enghien"), violating the sovereignty of [Baden](/wiki/Baden "Baden"). The Duke was quickly executed after a secret military trial, even though there was no proof he had been involved in the plot. Enghien's kidnapping and execution infuriated royalists and monarchs throughout Europe, and drew a formal protest from Russia.[[153]](#cite_note-159)[[154]](#cite_note-160)[[155]](#cite_note-161)

Following the royalist plot, Bonaparte's supporters convinced him that creating a hereditary regime would help secure it in case of his death, make it more acceptable to constitutional monarchists, and put it on the same footing as other European monarchies.[[156]](#cite_note-162)[[157]](#cite_note-163)[[158]](#cite_note-m2973-164) On 18 May, the senate proclaimed Napoleon *Emperor of the French* and approved a new constitution. The following day, Napoleon appointed 18 of his leading generals Marshals of the Empire.[[159]](#cite_note-165)

The hereditary empire was confirmed by a plebiscite in June. The official result showed 3.5 million voted "yes" and 2,569 voted "no". The yes count, however, was falsely inflated by 300,000 to 500,000 votes. The turnout, at 35%, was below the figure for the previous plebiscite.[[160]](#cite_note-166)[[161]](#cite_note-167) Britain, Russia, Sweden and the Ottoman Empire refused to recognize Napoleon's new title. Austria, however, recognized Napoleon as Emperor of the French in return for his recognition of [Francis I as Emperor of Austria](/wiki/Francis_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor "Francis II, Holy Roman Emperor").[[162]](#cite_note-168)

Napoleon's coronation, with the participation of [Pope Pius VII](/wiki/Pope_Pius_VII "Pope Pius VII"), took place at [Notre Dame de Paris](/wiki/Notre-Dame_de_Paris "Notre-Dame de Paris"), on 2 December 1804. After having been anointed by the pope, Napoleon crowned himself with a replica of [Charlemagne's](/wiki/Charlemagne "Charlemagne") crown. He then crowned Joséphine, who became only the second woman in French history, after [Marie de' Medici](/wiki/Marie_de%27_Medici "Marie de' Medici"), to be crowned and anointed. He then swore an oath to defend the territory of the Republic; to respect the Concordat, freedom of worship, political and civil liberty and the sale of nationalized lands; to raise no taxes except by law; to maintain the [Legion of Honour](/wiki/Legion_of_Honour "Legion of Honour"); and to govern in the interests, wellbeing and the glory of the French people.[[163]](#cite_note-169)

On 26 May, Napoleon crowned himself [King of Italy](/wiki/King_of_Italy "King of Italy"), with the [Iron Crown of Lombardy](/wiki/Iron_Crown_of_Lombardy "Iron Crown of Lombardy"), at the [Cathedral of Milan](/wiki/Milan_Cathedral "Milan Cathedral"). Austria saw this as a provocation because of its own territorial interests in Italy. When Napoleon incorporated [Genoa](/wiki/Genoa "Genoa") and [Liguria](/wiki/Ligurian_Republic "Ligurian Republic") into his empire, Austria formally protested against this violation of the Treaty of Lunéville.[[164]](#cite_note-170)

#### War of the Third Coalition

Main article: [War of the Third Coalition](/wiki/War_of_the_Third_Coalition "War of the Third Coalition")

By September 1805, Sweden, Russia, Austria, Naples and the Ottoman Empire had joined Britain in a coalition against France.[[165]](#cite_note-171)[[166]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013190-172)

In 1803 and 1804, Napoleon had assembled a force around [Boulogne](/wiki/Boulogne-sur-Mer "Boulogne-sur-Mer") for an invasion of Britain. They never invaded, but the force formed the core of Napoleon's *[Grande Armée](/wiki/Grande_Arm%C3%A9e "Grande Armée")*, created in August 1805.[[167]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200496-173)[[168]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer1984138-174) At the start, this French army had about 200,000 men organized into seven [corps](/wiki/Corps "Corps"), artillery and cavalry reserves, and the élite Imperial Guard.[[169]](#cite_note-175)[[168]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer1984138-174) By August 1805, the *Grande Armée* had grown to a force of 350,000 men,[[170]](#cite_note-176) who were well equipped, well trained, and led by competent officers.[[171]](#cite_note-177)

To facilitate the invasion, Napoleon planned to lure the Royal Navy from the English Channel by a diversionary attack on the [British West Indies](/wiki/British_West_Indies "British West Indies").[[172]](#cite_note-178) However, the plan unravelled after the British victory at the [Battle of Cape Finisterre](/wiki/Battle_of_Cape_Finisterre_(1805) "Battle of Cape Finisterre (1805)") in July 1805. French [Admiral Villeneuve](/wiki/Pierre-Charles_Villeneuve "Pierre-Charles Villeneuve") then retreated to [Cádiz](/wiki/C%C3%A1diz "Cádiz") instead of linking up with French naval forces at Brest for an attack on the English Channel.[[173]](#cite_note-179)

Facing a potential invasion from his continental enemies, Napoleon abandoned his invasion of England and sought to destroy the isolated Austrian armies in Southern Germany before their Russian ally could arrive in force. On 25 September, 200,000 French troops began to cross the Rhine on a front of 260 km (160 mi).[[174]](#cite_note-180)[[175]](#cite_note-181)

Austrian commander [Karl Mack](/wiki/Karl_Mack_von_Leiberich "Karl Mack von Leiberich") had gathered most of the Austrian army at the fortress of [Ulm](/wiki/Ulm "Ulm") in [Swabia](/wiki/Swabia "Swabia"). Napoleon's army, however, moved quickly and outflanked the Austrian positions. After some minor engagements that culminated in the [Battle of Ulm](/wiki/Battle_of_Ulm "Battle of Ulm"), Mack surrendered. For just 2,000 French casualties, Napoleon had captured 60,000 Austrian soldiers through his army's rapid marching.[[176]](#cite_note-182)

For the French, this spectacular victory on land was soured by the decisive victory that the Royal Navy attained at the [Battle of Trafalgar](/wiki/Battle_of_Trafalgar "Battle of Trafalgar") on 21 October. After Trafalgar, the Royal Navy was never again seriously challenged by Napoleon's fleet.[[177]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGlover1967233–252-183)

French forces occupied [Vienna](/wiki/Vienna "Vienna") in November, capturing 100,000 muskets, 500 cannons, and the intact bridges across the Danube.[[178]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChandler1973407-184) Napoleon then sent his army north in pursuit of the Allies. Tsar [Alexander I of Russia](/wiki/Alexander_I_of_Russia "Alexander I of Russia") and Francis I decided to engage Napoleon in battle, despite reservations from some of their subordinates.[[179]](#cite_note-Warfare-185)

At the [Battle of Austerlitz](/wiki/Battle_of_Austerlitz "Battle of Austerlitz"), on 2 December, Napoleon deployed his army below the Pratzen Heights. He ordered his right wing to feign retreat, enticing the Allies to descend from the heights in pursuit. The French centre and left wing then captured the heights and caught the allies in a pincer movement. Thousands of Russian troops fled across a frozen lake to escape the trap and 100 to 2,000 of them drowned.[[179]](#cite_note-Warfare-185)[[180]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013204–05-186) About a third of the allied forces were killed, captured or wounded.[[181]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer198418-187)

The disaster at Austerlitz led Austria to seek an armistice. By the subsequent Treaty of Pressburg, signed on 26 December, Austria left the coalition, lost substantial territory to the [Kingdom of Italy](/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy_(Napoleonic) "Kingdom of Italy (Napoleonic)") and Bavaria, and was forced to pay an indemnity of 40 million francs. Alexander's army was granted safe passage back to Russia.[[182]](#cite_note-Schom2-188)[[183]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013209-189)

Napoleon went on to say, "The battle of Austerlitz is the finest of all I have fought".[[182]](#cite_note-Schom2-188) [Frank McLynn](/wiki/Frank_McLynn "Frank McLynn") suggests that Napoleon was so successful at Austerlitz that he lost touch with reality, and what used to be French foreign policy became a "personal Napoleonic one".[[184]](#cite_note-190) [Vincent Cronin](/wiki/Vincent_Cronin "Vincent Cronin") disagrees, stating that Napoleon was not overly ambitious for himself, "he embodied the ambitions of thirty million Frenchmen".[[185]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECronin1994344-191)

#### Middle-Eastern alliances

Main articles: [Franco-Ottoman alliance](/wiki/Franco-Ottoman_alliance "Franco-Ottoman alliance") and [Franco-Persian alliance](/wiki/Franco-Persian_alliance "Franco-Persian alliance")

Napoleon continued to entertain a grand scheme to establish a French presence in the Middle East in order to put pressure on Britain and Russia, possibly by forming an alliance with the [Ottoman Empire](/wiki/Ottoman_Empire "Ottoman Empire").[[93]](#cite_note-Watson-98) In February 1806, Ottoman Emperor [Selim III](/wiki/Selim_III "Selim III") recognized Napoleon as *Emperor*. He also opted for an alliance with France, calling France "our sincere and natural ally".[[186]](#cite_note-192) That decision brought the Ottoman Empire into a losing war against Russia and Britain. A Franco-Persian alliance was formed between Napoleon and the [Persian Empire](/wiki/Qajar_dynasty "Qajar dynasty") of [Fat′h-Ali Shah Qajar](/wiki/Fath-Ali_Shah_Qajar "Fath-Ali Shah Qajar"). It collapsed in 1807 when France and Russia formed an unexpected alliance.[[93]](#cite_note-Watson-98) In the end, Napoleon made no effective alliances in the Middle East.[[187]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESicker200199-193)

#### War of the Fourth Coalition and Tilsit

Main article: [War of the Fourth Coalition](/wiki/War_of_the_Fourth_Coalition "War of the Fourth Coalition")

After Austerlitz, Napoleon increased his political power in Europe. In 1806, he deposed the [Bourbon king of Naples](/wiki/Ferdinand_I_of_the_Two_Sicilies "Ferdinand I of the Two Sicilies") and installed his elder brother, Joseph, on the throne. He then made his younger brother, Louis, King of Holland.[[188]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013216–20-194) He also established the [Confederation of the Rhine](/wiki/Confederation_of_the_Rhine "Confederation of the Rhine"), a collection of German states intended to serve as a buffer zone between France and Central Europe. The creation of the confederation spelled the end of the [Holy Roman Empire](/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire "Holy Roman Empire").[[189]](#cite_note-195)

Napoleon's growing influence in Germany threatened the status of Prussia as a great power and in response [Frederick William III](/wiki/Frederick_William_III_of_Prussia "Frederick William III of Prussia") decided on war with France. Prussia and Russia signed a new military alliance creating the fourth coalition against France. Prussia, however, committed a strategic blunder by declaring war when French troops were still in southern Germany and months before sufficient Russian troops could reach the front.[[190]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013224–25-196)

Napoleon invaded Prussia with 180,000 troops, rapidly marching on the right bank of the [River Saale](/wiki/Saale "Saale"). Upon learning the whereabouts of the Prussian army, the French swung westwards thus cutting the Prussians off from Berlin and the slowly approaching Russians. At the twin [battles of Jena and Auerstedt](/wiki/Battle_of_Jena%E2%80%93Auerstedt "Battle of Jena–Auerstedt"), fought on 14 October, the French convincingly defeated the Prussians and inflicted heavy casualties. With several major commanders dead or incapacitated, the Prussian king proved incapable of effectively commanding the army, which quickly disintegrated.[[191]](#cite_note-Brooks-2000-197)[[192]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013225–228-198)

In the following month, the French captured 140,000 soldiers and over 2,000 cannon. Despite their overwhelming defeat, the Prussians refused to negotiate with the French until the Russians had an opportunity to enter the fight.[[191]](#cite_note-Brooks-2000-197)[[193]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChandler1966467–468-199)[[194]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013233–34-200)

Following his triumph, Napoleon imposed the first elements of the [Continental System](/wiki/Continental_System "Continental System") through the [Berlin Decree](/wiki/Berlin_Decree "Berlin Decree") issued in November 1806. The Continental System, which prohibited European nations from trading with Britain, was widely violated throughout his reign.[[195]](#cite_note-201)

In the next few months, Napoleon marched against the advancing Russian armies through Poland and fought a bloody stalemate at the [Battle of Eylau](/wiki/Battle_of_Eylau "Battle of Eylau") in February 1807.[[196]](#cite_note-202) After a period of rest and consolidation on both sides, the war restarted in June with an initial struggle at [Heilsberg](/wiki/Battle_of_Heilsberg "Battle of Heilsberg") that proved indecisive.[[197]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013243-203)

On 14 June Napoleon obtained an overwhelming victory over the Russians at the [Battle of Friedland](/wiki/Battle_of_Friedland "Battle of Friedland"), inflicting casualties of up to 30% of the Russian army.[[198]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013244-204) The scale of their defeat convinced the Russians to make peace with the French. The two emperors began peace negotiations on 25 June at the town of [Tilsit](/wiki/Sovetsk,_Kaliningrad_Oblast "Sovetsk, Kaliningrad Oblast") during a meeting on a raft floating in the middle of the [River Niemen](/wiki/Neman "Neman") which separated the French and Russian troops and their respective spheres of influence.[[199]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013245–47-205)

Napoleon offered Alexander relatively lenient terms—demanding that Russia join the Continental System, withdraw its forces from [Wallachia](/wiki/Wallachia "Wallachia") and [Moldavia](/wiki/Moldavia "Moldavia"), and hand over the [Ionian Islands](/wiki/Ionian_Islands "Ionian Islands") to France. In contrast, Prussia was treated harshly. It lost half its territory and population and underwent a two-year occupation costing it about 1.4 billion francs. From former Prussian territory, Napoleon created the [Kingdom of Westphalia](/wiki/Kingdom_of_Westphalia "Kingdom of Westphalia"), ruled by his young brother Jérôme, and the [Duchy of Warsaw](/wiki/Duchy_of_Warsaw "Duchy of Warsaw").[[200]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts2014458–461-206)[[201]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013247–50-207)

Prussia's humiliating treatment at Tilsit caused lasting resentment against France in that country. The treaty was also unpopular in Russia, putting pressure on Alexander to end the alliance with France. Nevertheless, the [Treaties of Tilsit](/wiki/Treaties_of_Tilsit "Treaties of Tilsit") gave Napoleon a respite from war and allowed him to return to France, which he had not seen in over 300 days.[[200]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts2014458–461-206)[[202]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013251–53-208)

#### Peninsular War and Erfurt

Main article: [Peninsular War](/wiki/Peninsular_War "Peninsular War")

After Tilsit, Napoleon turned his attention to [Portugal](/wiki/Kingdom_of_Portugal "Kingdom of Portugal"), which was reluctant to strictly enforce the blockade against its traditional ally Britain.[[203]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013261–62-209)[[204]](#cite_note-210) On 17 October 1807, 24,000 French troops under General Junot crossed the Pyrenees with Spanish consent and headed towards Portugal to enforce the blockade.[[205]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFremont-BarnesFisher2004197-211) Junot occupied Lisbon in November, but the Portuguese royal family had already fled to Brazil with the Portuguese fleet.[[206]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013262–63-212)

In March 1808, a [palace coup](/wiki/Tumult_of_Aranjuez "Tumult of Aranjuez") led to the abdication of the Spanish king [Carlos IV](/wiki/Charles_IV_of_Spain "Charles IV of Spain") in favour of his son [Fernando VII](/wiki/Ferdinand_VII_of_Spain "Ferdinand VII of Spain").[[207]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFremont-BarnesFisher2004198–199-213)[[208]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013264-214) The following month, Napoleon summoned Carlos and Fernando to Bayonne where, in May, he forced them both to [relinquish](/wiki/Abdications_of_Bayonne "Abdications of Bayonne") their claims to the Spanish throne. Napoleon then made his brother Joseph King of Spain.[[209]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013269–70-215)

By then, there were 120,000 French troops garrisoned in the peninsula[[210]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFremont-BarnesFisher2004199-216)[[211]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013267-217) and widespread Spanish opposition to the occupation and the overthrow of the Spanish Bourbons. On 2 May, an [uprising](/wiki/Dos_de_Mayo_Uprising "Dos de Mayo Uprising") against the French broke out in Madrid and spread throughout Spain in the following weeks. In the face of brutal French repression, the uprising developed into a sustained conflict.[[212]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013271-72,_275-218)

In July, Joseph travelled to Madrid where he was proclaimed King of Spain on the 24th. However, following news of a French defeat by regular Spanish forces at the [Battle of Bailén](/wiki/Battle_of_Bail%C3%A9n "Battle of Bailén"), Joseph fled Madrid several days later.[[213]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013276–78-219) The following month, a British force landed in Portugal and, on the 21st, they defeated the French at [Vimiero](/wiki/Battle_of_Vimeiro "Battle of Vimeiro"). Under the [Convention of Cintra](/wiki/Convention_of_Cintra "Convention of Cintra"), the French evacuated Portugal.[[214]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013296-220)[[215]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer1984218-221)

The defeats at Bailén and Vimiero convinced Napoleon that he had to take command of the Iberian campaign. Before leaving for Spain, he attempted to strengthen the alliance with Russia and obtain a commitment from Alexander that Russia would declare war on Austria if she attacked France. At the [Congress of Erfurt](/wiki/Congress_of_Erfurt "Congress of Erfurt") in October 1808, Napoleon and Alexander reached an agreement that recognized the Russian conquest of [Finland](/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Finland "Grand Duchy of Finland") and called upon Britain to cease its war against France.[[216]](#cite_note-222) However, Alexander failed to provide a firm commitment to make war with Austria.[[217]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013286-223)[[218]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer1984118-224)

On 6 November, Napoleon was in [Vitoria](/wiki/Vitoria-Gasteiz "Vitoria-Gasteiz") and took command of 240,000 French troops. After a series of victories over Anglo-Spanish forces, they retook Madrid on 4 December.[[219]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFremont-BarnesFisher2004205-225) Napoleon then pursued the retreating British forces who were eventually evacuated at [Corunna](/wiki/Battle_of_Corunna "Battle of Corunna") in January 1809. Napoleon left for France on 17 January, leaving Joseph in command.[[220]](#cite_note-226)[[221]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013296–300-227)

Napoleon never returned to Spain after the 1808 campaign. In April, the British sent another army to the peninsula under Arthur Wellesley, the future [Duke of Wellington](/wiki/Arthur_Wellesley,_1st_Duke_of_Wellington "Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington"). British, Portuguese and Spanish regular forces engaged the French in a protracted series of conflicts. Meanwhile, a brutal guerrilla war engulfed much of the Spanish countryside, a conflict in which atrocities were committed by both sides.[[222]](#cite_note-Chandler-1966b-228)[[215]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer1984218-221)

Napoleon later called the Peninsular campaign, "the unlucky war [that] ruined me."[[223]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner2004128-229) It tied up some 300,000 French troops from 1808 to 1812. By 1814, the French had been driven from the peninsula, with over 150,000 casualties in the campaign.[[222]](#cite_note-Chandler-1966b-228)[[224]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell201578–80-230)

#### War of the Fifth Coalition

Main article: [War of the Fifth Coalition](/wiki/War_of_the_Fifth_Coalition "War of the Fifth Coalition")

The overthrow of the Spanish Bourbons caused alarm in Austria over Napoleon's ambitions while France's military difficulties in the Peninsular encouraged Austria to go to war.[[225]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013304–05-231)[[226]](#cite_note-232) In the early morning of 10 April 1809, the Austrian army crossed the [Inn River](/wiki/Inn_(river) "Inn (river)") and invaded Bavaria. The Austrian advance, however, was disorganized and they were unable to defeat the Bavarian army before the French could concentrate their forces.[[227]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013306-233) Napoleon arrived from Paris on the 17th to lead the French campaign. In the following [Battle of Eckmühl](/wiki/Battle_of_Eckm%C3%BChl "Battle of Eckmühl") he was slightly wounded in the heel but the Austrians were forced to retreat across the Danube. The French occupied Vienna on 13 May but most of the population had fled and the retreating army had destroyed all four bridges across the river.[[228]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013306–08-234)

On 21 May, the French attempted to cross the Danube, precipitating the [Battle of Aspern-Essling](/wiki/Battle_of_Aspern-Essling "Battle of Aspern-Essling"). Both sides inflicted about 23,000 casualties on each other and the French were forced back.[[229]](#cite_note-235) The battle was reported in European capitals as a defeat for Napoleon and damaged his aura of invincibility.[[230]](#cite_note-236)[[231]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013308–12-237)

After six weeks of preparations, Napoleon made another attempt at crossing the Danube.[[232]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChandler1973708-238) In the ensuing [Battle of Wagram](/wiki/Battle_of_Wagram "Battle of Wagram") (5–6 July) the Austrians were forced to retreat but the French and Austrians each suffered losses of 37,000 to 39,000 killed, wounded or captured.[[233]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013312–14-239)[[234]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChandler1973729-240) The French caught up with the retreating Austrians at [Znaim](/wiki/Znojmo "Znojmo") on 10 July, and the latter signed an [armistice](/wiki/Armistice_of_Znaim "Armistice of Znaim") on the 12th.[[235]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013314-241)

In August, a British force [landed in Holland](/wiki/Walcheren_Campaign "Walcheren Campaign") but lost 4,000 men, mainly to illness, before withdrawing in December.[[236]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer1984285–86-242)

The [Treaty of Schönbrunn](/wiki/Treaty_of_Sch%C3%B6nbrunn "Treaty of Schönbrunn") in October 1809 was harsh for Austria which lost substantial territory and over three million subjects.[[237]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChandler1973732-243) France received [Carinthia](/wiki/Carinthia_(Slovenia) "Carinthia (Slovenia)"), [Carniola](/wiki/Carniola "Carniola"), and the Adriatic ports of Trieste and Fiume(Rijeka); the part of Poland annexed by Austria in the [third partition](/wiki/Third_partition_of_Poland "Third partition of Poland") in 1795, known at the time as West Galicia, was given to the Polish-ruled [Duchy of Warsaw](/wiki/Duchy_of_Warsaw "Duchy of Warsaw"); and the territory of the former Archbishopric of Salzburg went to Bavaria.[[238]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFremont-BarnesFisher2004144-244) Austria was required to pay an indemnity of 200 million francs and its army was reduced to 150,000 men.[[239]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013316-245)

#### Consolidation of the Empire

Napoleon's union with Joséphine had not produced a child, and he decided to secure the dynasty and strengthen its position in Europe by a strategic marriage into one of Europe's major royal houses. In November 1809, he announced his decision to divorce Joséphine and the marriage was annulled in January 1810.[[240]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013321–25-246) Napoleon had already commenced negotiations for the marriage of Tsar Alexander's sister Anna, but the Tsar responded that she was too young. Napoleon then turned to Austria, and a marriage to the Austrian Emperor's daughter, Marie Louise, was quickly agreed.[[241]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013326–330-247)

The marriage was formalized in a civil ceremony on 1 April and a religious service at the Louvre on the following day. The marriage to Marie Louise was widely seen as a shift in French policy towards stronger ties with Austria and away from the already strained relationship with Russia.[[242]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013328-30-248) On 20 March 1811, Marie Louise gave birth to the heir apparent, [François Charles Joseph Napoleon](/wiki/Napoleon_II "Napoleon II"), King of Rome.[[243]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013334–41-249)

With the annexation of the Papal states (May 1809, February 1810), Holland (July 1810) and the northern coastal regions of Westphalia (August 1810), mainland France further increased its territory. Napoleon now ruled about 40% of the European population either directly or indirectly through his satellite kingdoms.[[244]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013350–53-250)

#### Invasion of Russia

Main article: [French invasion of Russia](/wiki/French_invasion_of_Russia "French invasion of Russia")

Tsar Alexander saw the creation of the Grand Duchy of Warsaw, Napoleon's marriage alliance with Austria and the election of the French Marshal [Bernadotte](/wiki/Charles_XIV_John "Charles XIV John") as Crown Prince of Sweden as attempts to contain Russia. In December 1810, Napoleon annexed the [Duchy of Oldenburg](/wiki/Duchy_of_Oldenburg "Duchy of Oldenburg") which Alexander considered an insult as his uncle was the duke. The Tsar responded by allowing neutral shipping into Russian ports and banning most French imports. Russia feared that Napoleon intended to restore the Kingdom of Poland while Napoleon suspected Russia of seeking an alliance with Britain against France.[[245]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013353–55-251)[[246]](#cite_note-McLynn-1997-252)

In late 1811, Napoleon began planning an invasion of Russia. A Franco-Prussian alliance signed in February 1812 forced Prussia to provide 20,000 troops for the invasion and, in March, Austria agreed to provide 30,000 men.[[247]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013358–61-253)[[248]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLynn1997501-254) Napoleon's multinational *grande armée* comprised around 450,000 frontline troops of which about a third were native French speakers. Napoleon called the invasion the "Second Polish War," but he refused to guarantee an independent Poland for fear of alienating his Austrian and Prussian allies.[[249]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013361,_370–71-255)[[250]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLynn1997508-256)[[251]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEsdaile2007563–64-257)

On 24 June, Napoleon's troops began crossing the [Nieman river](/wiki/Neman "Neman") into [Russian Lithuania](/wiki/Lithuania "Lithuania") with the aim of luring the Russians into one or two decisive battles.[[252]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013370-258) The Russians retreated 320 kilometres east to the [Dvina](/wiki/Daugava "Daugava") river and implemented a [scorched earth policy](/wiki/Scorched_earth "Scorched earth"), making it increasingly difficult for the French to forage food for themselves and their horses.[[253]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey2007773-259)[[254]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013371-72-260) On 18 August, Napoleon captured [Smolensk](/wiki/Battle_of_Smolensk_(1812) "Battle of Smolensk (1812)") with the loss of 9,000 of his men, but the Russians were able to withdraw in good order.[[255]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013379–82-261)

The Russians, now commanded by Kutuzov, made a stand at [Borodino](/wiki/Battle_of_Borodino "Battle of Borodino"), outside Moscow, on 7 September. The battle resulted in 44,000 Russian and 35,000 French dead, wounded or captured, in one of the bloodiest days of battle in Europe up to that time.[[256]](#cite_note-262)[[257]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013385-263) The Russians withdrew overnight and Napoleon later stated, "The most terrible of all my battles was the one before Moscow. The French showed themselves worthy of victory, and the Russians worthy of being invincible".[[258]](#cite_note-264)

The Russians retreated to [Tarutino](/wiki/Tarutino,_Russia "Tarutino, Russia"), and Napoleon entered Moscow on 14 September. The following evening, the city was set on fire on the orders of its governor [Feodor Rostopchin](/wiki/Fyodor_Rostopchin "Fyodor Rostopchin"). Alexander, in [St Petersburg](/wiki/Saint_Petersburg "Saint Petersburg"), refused to negotiate a peace, and after six weeks Napoleon's army evacuated Moscow.[[259]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013388–98-265)

After capturing [Maloyaroslavets](/wiki/Battle_of_Maloyaroslavets "Battle of Maloyaroslavets") with the loss of 4,000 to 10,000 men, Napoleon retreated towards Smolensk. The French were attacked by [Cossacks](/wiki/Cossacks "Cossacks") and peasants and suffered from the intense cold, disease and lack of food and water. Around 40,000 to 50,000 troops reached Smolensk on 9 November, a loss of about 60,000 in three weeks. Napoleon also heard that an [attempted coup](/wiki/Malet_coup_of_1812 "Malet coup of 1812") by General Malet in Paris had only narrowly failed.[[260]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013400–407-266)

From Smolensk, Napoleon's army headed for Vilnius, where there was a French garrison of 20,000. In late November, under attack from all sides by Russian forces, the *grande armée* managed to cross the [Berezina](/wiki/Battle_of_Berezina "Battle of Berezina") river on pontoon bridges in temperatures reaching −40 °C (−40 °F). On 5 December, shortly before arriving in Vilnius, Napoleon left his disintegrating army for Paris.[[261]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013410–19-267) In the following weeks, the remnants of the *grande armée*, about 75,000 troops, crossed the Nieman into allied territory. Russian military losses in the campaign were up to 300,000 and total military deaths from both sides were up to one million.[[262]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013425-268)

#### War of the Sixth Coalition

Main article: [War of the Sixth Coalition](/wiki/War_of_the_Sixth_Coalition "War of the Sixth Coalition")

The French, pursued by the Russians, withdrew from most of Poland and Prussia over the winter of 1812–13 while both sides rebuilt their forces.[[263]](#cite_note-269) Sweden and Prussia declared war on France in March 1813. In April, Napoleon assumed command of an army of 200,000 troops,[[264]](#cite_note-270)[[265]](#cite_note-271) and defeated the coalition at [Lützen](/wiki/Battle_of_L%C3%BCtzen_(1813) "Battle of Lützen (1813)") and [Bautzen](/wiki/Battle_of_Bautzen_(1813) "Battle of Bautzen (1813)").[[266]](#cite_note-272) Britain formally joined the coalition in June followed by Austria in August,[[267]](#cite_note-273) but the allies were again defeated in the [Battle of Dresden](/wiki/Battle_of_Dresden "Battle of Dresden") (August 1813).[[268]](#cite_note-274)

The coalition, however, had a growing advantage in infantry, cavalry, reserves and armaments. In the largest battle of the Napoleonic wars, the coalition was victorious at [Leipzig](/wiki/Battle_of_Leipzig "Battle of Leipzig") in October. Although coalition casualties were 54,000 men, the French lost 38,000 killed or wounded and 15,000 taken prisoner. Up to 50,000 more were lost to death, illness and desertion during the French retreat to the Rhine.[[269]](#cite_note-275)[[270]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChandler19951020-276)

The [Frankfurt proposals](/wiki/Frankfurt_proposals "Frankfurt proposals") were peace terms offered by the coalition in November 1813 under which Napoleon would remain emperor, but France would be reduced to its "natural frontiers." That meant that France would retain control of Belgium, Savoy and the west bank of the Rhine, while withdrawing from Spain, Holland, Italy and Germany. Napoleon did not accept the terms and the allies crossed the Rhine into French territory on 1 January 1814.[[271]](#cite_note-277) Wellington's British forces had already crossed the Pyrenees into south-western France.[[272]](#cite_note-278)

In north-eastern France, Napoleon led about 70,000 troops against a coalition army of 200,000. After a defeat at [La Rothière](/wiki/Battle_of_La_Rothi%C3%A8re "Battle of La Rothière"), the French won a series of victories in [February](/wiki/Six_Days%27_Campaign "Six Days' Campaign") which induced the coalition to offer peace on the basis of France's 1791 frontiers. Napoleon, however, decided to fight on.[[273]](#cite_note-279)[[274]](#cite_note-280)

After a series of battles in March, the allies forced Napoleon to retreat at the [Battle of Arcis-sur-Aube](/wiki/Battle_of_Arcis-sur-Aube "Battle of Arcis-sur-Aube") (20–21 March). The coalition then moved towards Paris, whose defence was under the command of Joseph Bonaparte.[[275]](#cite_note-281) On 29 March, a coalition army of 200,000 began their attack on the Belleville and Montmartre heights. Empress Marie Louise fled Paris that evening with her son, the King of Rome. With an army of only 38,000 to defend the capital, Joseph authorized the French marshal [Auguste de Marmont](/wiki/Auguste_de_Marmont "Auguste de Marmont") to capitulate on 31 March. The following day, the allies accepted [Charles Maurice de Talleyrand-Périgord](/wiki/Charles_Maurice_de_Talleyrand-P%C3%A9rigord "Charles Maurice de Talleyrand-Périgord") as head of a provisional government. On 2 April, the Senate deposed Napoleon.[[276]](#cite_note-282)

Meanwhile, Napoleon was in Fontainebleau with an army of 40,000 to 60,000. He contemplated a march on Paris but, on 4 April, his senior commanders persuaded him to abdicate in favour of his son, with Marie Louise as regent.[[g]](#cite_note-284) Tsar Alexander, however, demanded an unconditional abdication and Napoleon reluctantly complied on 6 April.[[278]](#cite_note-285)[[279]](#cite_note-286)[[280]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGates2003259-287)[[281]](#cite_note-288)

In his farewell address to the soldiers of the [Old Guard](/wiki/Old_Guard_(France) "Old Guard (France)") on 20 April, Napoleon said:

> "Soldiers of my Old Guard, I have come to bid you farewell. For twenty years you have accompanied me faithfully on the paths of honor and glory. ...With men like you, our cause was [not] lost, but the war would have dragged on interminably, and it would have been a civil war. ...
