So I am sacrificing our interests to those of our country. ...Do not lament my fate; if I have agreed to live on, it is to serve our glory. I wish to write the history of the great deeds we have done together. Farewell, my children!"[[282]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell201597-289)

### Exile to Elba

Main article: [Principality of Elba](/wiki/Principality_of_Elba "Principality of Elba")

With the [Treaty of Fontainebleau](/wiki/Treaty_of_Fontainebleau_(1814) "Treaty of Fontainebleau (1814)") of 11 April 1814, the allies exiled Napoleon to [Elba](/wiki/Elba "Elba"), an island of 12,000 inhabitants in the Mediterranean, 10 km (6 mi) off the Tuscan coast, where they made him [sovereign](/wiki/Principality_of_Elba "Principality of Elba"). The following night, Napoleon attempted suicide with poison he had carried after nearly being captured by the Russians during the retreat from Moscow. Its potency had weakened with age, however, and he survived to be exiled, while his wife and son took refuge in Austria.[[283]](#cite_note-290) He was conveyed to the island on [HMS *Undaunted*](/wiki/HMS_Undaunted_(1807) "HMS Undaunted (1807)") and disembarked at [Portoferraio](/wiki/Portoferraio "Portoferraio") on 4 May 1814. In the first few months on Elba, he drew up plans for administrative reforms, road and building works, and improvements to the island's mines and agriculture, but results were limited by lack of funds.[[284]](#cite_note-291)[[285]](#cite_note-292)[[286]](#cite_note-293) When Napoleon learned that Joséphine had died in France on 29 May, he was distraught and locked himself in his room for two days.[[287]](#cite_note-294)

Napoleon understood that the French king [Louis XVIII](/wiki/Louis_XVIII "Louis XVIII") was unpopular. Realizing that his wife and son would not be joining him in exile, cut off from the allowance guaranteed to him by the Treaty of Fontainebleau, and aware of rumours he was about to be banished to a remote island in the Atlantic Ocean, Napoleon escaped from Elba in the brig [*Inconstant*](/wiki/French_brig_Inconstant_(1811) "French brig Inconstant (1811)") on 26 February 1815 with about 1,000 men and a flotilla of seven vessels.[[288]](#cite_note-Mclynn6042-295)[[289]](#cite_note-296)

### Hundred Days

Main article: [Hundred Days](/wiki/Hundred_Days "Hundred Days")

On 1 March 1815, Napoleon and his followers landed on the French mainland at [Golfe-Juan](/wiki/Golfe-Juan "Golfe-Juan") and headed for [Grenoble](/wiki/Grenoble "Grenoble") through the foothills of the Alps, taking the route now known as [Route Napoléon](/wiki/Route_Napol%C3%A9on "Route Napoléon").[[288]](#cite_note-Mclynn6042-295)[[290]](#cite_note-297) The 5th Regiment intercepted him just south of [Grenoble](/wiki/Grenoble "Grenoble") on 7 March. Napoleon approached the battalion alone and called to them, "Here I am. Kill your Emperor, if you wish!" The soldiers responded with, "*Vive l'empereur!*" and joined Napoleon's men.[[291]](#cite_note-298)[[292]](#cite_note-299) 7 days later (March 14th), 6,000 troops under [Michel Ney](/wiki/Michel_Ney "Michel Ney"), who had boasted that he would bring Napoleon to Paris in an iron cage, also joined Napoleon.[[293]](#cite_note-300)

On 13 March, the powers at the [Congress of Vienna](/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna "Congress of Vienna") declared Napoleon an [outlaw](/wiki/Outlaw "Outlaw").[[294]](#cite_note-301) Four days later, Great Britain, Russia, Austria, and Prussia each pledged to put 150,000 men into the field to end his rule.[[295]](#cite_note-302) Louis XVIII, however, fled Paris for Belgium in the early hours of 20 March after realizing that he did not have enough reliable troops to oppose Napoleon. Napoleon entered Paris that evening.[[296]](#cite_note-303)

Napoleon appointed a government and introduced constitutional changes which were approved by plebiscite in May. A Chamber of Representatives was also indirectly elected that month on a highly restrictive property franchise.[[297]](#cite_note-304)[[298]](#cite_note-305) Napoleon's priority was to raise an army to face the coalition, but the law did not allow conscription and he was only able to raise about 300,000 men, mostly raw recruits and national guards.[[299]](#cite_note-306)

On 12 June, Napoleon led about 124,000 men, known as the [Army of the North](/wiki/Army_of_the_North_(France) "Army of the North (France)"), into Belgium, aiming to drive a wedge between Wellington's army of 112,000 British, German and Dutch troops and [Gebhard Leberecht von Blücher](/wiki/Gebhard_Leberecht_von_Bl%C3%BCcher "Gebhard Leberecht von Blücher")'s force of 130,000 Prussians and Saxons.[[300]](#cite_note-307)[[301]](#cite_note-308) After engagements at the [Battle of Ligny](/wiki/Battle_of_Ligny "Battle of Ligny") and [Battle of Quatre Bras](/wiki/Battle_of_Quatre_Bras "Battle of Quatre Bras"), Napoleon confronted Wellington at the [Battle of Waterloo](/wiki/Battle_of_Waterloo "Battle of Waterloo") on 18 June. Wellington's army withstood repeated attacks by the French until, late in the afternoon, Blücher's Prussians arrived in force on Napoleon's right flank. The coalition forces broke through Napoleon's lines, inflicting a devastating defeat.[[302]](#cite_note-309)

Napoleon returned to Paris and found that the legislature had turned against him. Realizing that his position was untenable, [he abdicated on 22 June in favour of his son](/wiki/Abdication_of_Napoleon,_1815 "Abdication of Napoleon, 1815"). He left Paris three days later and settled at Joséphine's former palace in [Château de Malmaison](/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Malmaison "Château de Malmaison").[[303]](#cite_note-310) By 28 June, the Prussian army was at [Senlis](/wiki/Senlis "Senlis"), just north of Paris.[[304]](#cite_note-311)

When Napoleon heard that Prussian troops had orders to capture him dead or alive, he fled to [Rochefort, Charente-Maritime](/wiki/Rochefort,_Charente-Maritime "Rochefort, Charente-Maritime"), considering an escape to the United States. However, when he found that British ships were blockading the port, he surrendered to [Frederick Lewis Maitland](/wiki/Frederick_Lewis_Maitland "Frederick Lewis Maitland") on [HMS *Bellerophon*](/wiki/HMS_Bellerophon_(1786) "HMS Bellerophon (1786)") on 15 July 1815.[[305]](#cite_note-312)[[306]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECordingly2004254-313)

Exile on Saint Helena
---------------------

Main article: [Napoleon I's exile to St. Helena](/wiki/Napoleon_I%27s_exile_to_St._Helena "Napoleon I's exile to St. Helena")

Napoleon was held in British custody and transferred to the island of [Saint Helena](/wiki/Saint_Helena "Saint Helena") in the Atlantic Ocean, 1,870 km (1,010 nmi) from the west coast of Africa. Napoleon and 27 followers arrived at Jamestown, Saint Helena, in October 1815 on board [HMS *Northumberland*](/wiki/HMS_Northumberland_(1798) "HMS Northumberland (1798)"). The prisoner was guarded by a garrison of 2,100 soldiers while a squadron of 10 ships continuously patrolled the waters to prevent escape.[[307]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer201813–34-314) In the following years, there were rumours of escape plots, but no serious attempts were made.[[308]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer201871–74-315)

Napoleon stayed for two months at a pavilion in [Briars](/wiki/Briars,_Saint_Helena "Briars, Saint Helena") before he was moved to [Longwood House](/wiki/Longwood_House "Longwood House"), a 40-room wooden bungalow. The location and interior of the house were damp, windswept, rat-infested and unhealthy.[[309]](#cite_note-316)[[310]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer201839–41,_90-317) *[The Times](/wiki/The_Times "The Times")* published articles insinuating the British government was trying to hasten his death. Napoleon often complained of his living conditions in letters to the island's governor, [Hudson Lowe](/wiki/Hudson_Lowe "Hudson Lowe"),[[311]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchom1997769–770-318) while his attendants complained of "colds, [catarrhs](/wiki/Catarrh "Catarrh"), damp floors and poor provisions".[[312]](#cite_note-319)

Napoleon insisted on imperial formality. When he held a dinner party, men were expected to wear military dress and "women [appeared] in evening gowns and gems. It was an explicit denial of the circumstances of his captivity".[[313]](#cite_note-320)[[314]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer201844–46,_64–67-321) He formally received visitors, read, and dictated his memoirs and commentaries on military campaigns.[[315]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer201843–44-322) He studied English under [Emmanuel, comte de Las Cases](/wiki/Emmanuel,_comte_de_Las_Cases "Emmanuel, comte de Las Cases") for a few months but gave up as he was poor at languages.[[316]](#cite_note-323)[[317]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer201841-324)

Napoleon also circulated reports of poor treatment in the hope that public opinion would force the allies to revoke his exile on Saint Helena.[[318]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer201864–67-325) Under instructions from the British government, Lowe cut Napoleon's expenditure, refused to recognize him as a former emperor, and made his supporters sign a guarantee they would stay with the prisoner indefinitely.[[319]](#cite_note-Lowe2-326)[[318]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer201864–67-325) Accounts of the mistreatment led, in March 1817, to a debate in parliament and [Henry Vassall-Fox, 3rd Baron Holland](/wiki/Henry_Vassall-Fox,_3rd_Baron_Holland "Henry Vassall-Fox, 3rd Baron Holland")'s call for an inquiry.[[320]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer201864-327)

| Status: Repealed | |

In mid-1817, Napoleon's health worsened. His physician, [Barry O'Meara](/wiki/Barry_O%27Meara "Barry O'Meara"), diagnosed chronic [hepatitis](/wiki/Hepatitis "Hepatitis") and warned Lowe that he could die from the poor climate and lack of exercise. Lowe thought O'Meara was exaggerating and dismissed him in July 1818.[[321]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer201893–97-328)

In November 1818, the allies announced that Napoleon would remain a prisoner on Saint Helena for life. When he learnt the news, he became depressed and more isolated, spending longer periods in his rooms which further undermined his health.[[322]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2018103–105-329)[[323]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamoyski2018638–639-330) A number of his entourage also left Saint Helena including Las Cases in December 1816, General Gaspard Gourgaud in March 1818 and [Albine de Montholon](/wiki/Albine_de_Montholon "Albine de Montholon"), who was possibly Napoleon's lover, in July 1819.[[324]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer201882–89,_90–93-331)

In September 1819, two priests and a new physician, [François Carlo Antommarchi](/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Carlo_Antommarchi "François Carlo Antommarchi"), joined Napoleon's retinue.[[325]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2018105-332)

### Death

See also: [Death mask of Napoleon](/wiki/Death_mask_of_Napoleon "Death mask of Napoleon"), [Retour des cendres](/wiki/Retour_des_cendres "Retour des cendres"), [Napoleon's tomb](/wiki/Napoleon%27s_tomb "Napoleon's tomb"), and [Death of Napoleon I](/wiki/Death_of_Napoleon_I "Death of Napoleon I")

Napoleon's health continued to worsen, and in March 1821 he was confined to bed. In April he wrote two wills declaring that he had been murdered by the British, that the Bourbons would fall and that his son would rule France. He left his fortune to 97 legatees and asked to be buried by the Seine.[[326]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2018108–13-333)

On 3 May he was given the [last rites](/wiki/Last_rites "Last rites") but could not take communion due to his illness.[[327]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2018115-334) He died on 5 May 1821 at age 51. His last words, variously recorded by those present, were either *France, l'armée, tête d'armée, Joséphine* ("France, the army, head of the army, Joséphine"),[[328]](#cite_note-McLynn6552-335)[[329]](#cite_note-336) or *qui recule*...*à la tête d'armée* ("who retreats... at the head of the army")[[330]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2018115,_282n82-337) or "France, my son, the Army."[[330]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2018115,_282n82-337)

Antommarchi and the British wrote separate autopsy reports, each concluding that Napoleon had died of internal bleeding caused by [stomach cancer](/wiki/Stomach_cancer "Stomach cancer"), the disease that had killed his father.[[331]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2018120–23-338)[[332]](#cite_note-Lugli-2021-339) A later theory, based on high concentrations of arsenic found in samples of Napoleon's hair, held that Napoleon had died of [arsenic poisoning](/wiki/Arsenic_poisoning "Arsenic poisoning"). However, subsequent studies also found high concentrations of arsenic in hair samples from Napoleon's childhood and from his son and Joséphine. Arsenic was widely used in medicines and products such as hair creams in the 19th century.[[333]](#cite_note-cullen1562-340)[[334]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHindmarshSavory20082092-341) A 2021 study by an international team of gastrointestinal pathologists once again concluded that Napoleon died of stomach cancer.[[332]](#cite_note-Lugli-2021-339)

Napoleon was buried with military honours in the [Valley of the Geraniums](/wiki/Valley_of_the_Tomb "Valley of the Tomb").[[335]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2018126–27-342)[[328]](#cite_note-McLynn6552-335) Napoleon's heart and intestines were removed and sealed inside his coffin. [Napoleon's penis](/wiki/Napoleon%27s_penis "Napoleon's penis") was allegedly removed during the autopsy and sold and exhibited. In 1840, the British government gave [Louis Philippe I](/wiki/Louis_Philippe_I "Louis Philippe I") permission to return Napoleon's remains to France. Napoleon's body was exhumed and found to be well preserved as it had been sealed in four coffins (two of metal and two of mahogany) and placed in a masonry tomb.[[336]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2018141,_195–99-343) On 15 December 1840, a [state funeral](/wiki/State_funeral "State funeral") was held in Paris with 700,000-1,000,000 attendees who lined the route of the funeral procession to the chapel of [Les Invalides](/wiki/Les_Invalides "Les Invalides"). The coffin was later placed in the cupola in St Jérôme's Chapel, where it remained until [Napoleon's tomb](/wiki/Napoleon%27s_tomb "Napoleon's tomb"), designed by [Louis Visconti](/wiki/Louis_Visconti "Louis Visconti"), was completed.[[337]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2018216–19,_225-344) In 1861, during the reign of [Napoleon III](/wiki/Napoleon_III "Napoleon III"), his remains were entombed in a [sarcophagus](/wiki/Sarcophagus "Sarcophagus") in the crypt under the dome at Les Invalides.[[338]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2018235-345)

Religion
--------

Further information: [Napoleon and the Catholic Church](/wiki/Napoleon_and_the_Catholic_Church "Napoleon and the Catholic Church")

### Religious beliefs

Napoleon was baptized in Ajaccio on 21 July 1771, and raised a Roman Catholic. He began to question his faith at age 13 while at Brienne.[[339]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis1997a239–41-346) Biographers have variously described him from that time as a [deist](/wiki/Deism "Deism"), a follower of Rousseau's "natural religion" or a believer in destiny. He consistently expressed his belief in a God or creator.[[340]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis1997a236-347)

He understood the power of organized religion in social and political affairs, and later sought to use it to support his regime.[[341]](#cite_note-348)[[342]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis1997a236–37-349) His attitude to religion is often described as utilitarian.[[343]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis1997a235-350)[[344]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer201384-351) In 1800 he stated, "it was by making myself a Catholic that I won the war in the Vendée, by making myself a Moslem that I established myself in Egypt, by making myself an [ultramontane](/wiki/Ultramontanism "Ultramontanism") that I turned men's hearts towards me in Italy. If I were to govern a nation of Jews I would rebuild the [Temple of Solomon](/wiki/Temple_in_Jerusalem "Temple in Jerusalem")."[[343]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis1997a235-350)

Napoleon had a [civil marriage](/wiki/Civil_marriage "Civil marriage") with Joséphine in 1796 and, at the pope's insistence, a private religious ceremony with her the day before his coronation as Emperor in 1804. This marriage was annulled by tribunals under Napoleon's control in January 1810.[[345]](#cite_note-352) In April 1810, Napoleon married Austrian princess Marie Louise in a Catholic ceremony. Napoleon was [excommunicated](/wiki/Excommunicated "Excommunicated") by the pope through the bull *[Quum memoranda](/wiki/Quum_memoranda "Quum memoranda")* in 1809.[[346]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis1997a248-353) His will in 1821 stated, "I die in the Apostolical Roman religion, in the bosom of which I was born, more than fifty years since."[[347]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner2004197-354)

Napoleon read the [Koran](/wiki/Koran "Koran") in translation and had an interest in [Islam](/wiki/Islam "Islam") and the [Orient](/wiki/Orient "Orient").[[348]](#cite_note-355) He also defended [Muhammad](/wiki/Muhammad "Muhammad") ("a great man") against Voltaire's *[Mahomet](/wiki/Mahomet_(play) "Mahomet (play)")*.[[349]](#cite_note-356)

### Concordat

Further information: [Concordat of 1801](/wiki/Concordat_of_1801 "Concordat of 1801")

Seeking national reconciliation between revolutionaries and Catholics, Napoleon and [Pope Pius VII](/wiki/Pope_Pius_VII "Pope Pius VII") agreed to the [Concordat of 1801](/wiki/Concordat_of_1801 "Concordat of 1801"). The agreement recognized the Catholic Church as the majority church of France and in return the Church recognized Napoleon's regime, undercutting much of the ground from royalists. The Concordat confirmed the seizure of Church lands and endowments during the revolution, but reintroduced state salaries for the clergy. The government also controlled the nomination of bishops for investiture by the pope. Bishops and other clergy were required to swear an oath of loyalty to the regime.[[350]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis1997a244–45-357)[[351]](#cite_note-358)[[352]](#cite_note-359)

When the Concordat was published on 8 April 1802, Napoleon presented another set of laws called the [Organic Articles](/wiki/Organic_Articles "Organic Articles"), which further increased state control over the French Church.[[350]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis1997a244–45-357) Similar arrangements were made with the Church in territories controlled by Napoleon, especially in Italy and Germany.[[353]](#cite_note-360)

### Arrest of Pope Pius VII

Napoleon progressively occupied and annexed the [Papal States](/wiki/Papal_States "Papal States") from 1805. When he annexed Rome in May 1809, the pope excommunicated him the following month. In July, French officials arrested the pope in the Vatican and exiled him to [Savona](/wiki/Savona "Savona"). In 1812 the pontiff was transferred to the [Palace of Fontainebleau](/wiki/Palace_of_Fontainebleau "Palace of Fontainebleau") in France.[[354]](#cite_note-361) In January 1813, Napoleon pressured the pope to sign a new "Concordat of Fontainebleau" which was soon repudiated by the pontiff. The pope was not released until 1814.[[346]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis1997a248-353)

### Religious emancipation

Further information: [Napoleon and the Jews](/wiki/Napoleon_and_the_Jews "Napoleon and the Jews") and [Napoleon and Protestants](/wiki/Napoleon_and_Protestants "Napoleon and Protestants")

In February 1795, the National Convention proclaimed religious equality for France's Protestant churches and other religions. In April 1802, Napoleon published laws increasing state control of Calvinist congregations and Lutheran directories, with their pastors to be paid by the state.[[355]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis1997a242,_245-362) With Napoleon's military victories, formal religious equality and civil rights for religious minorities spread to the conquered territories and [satellite states](/wiki/Satellite_state "Satellite state"), although their implementation varied with the local authorities.[[356]](#cite_note-McLynn-1997a-363)

The Jews of France had been granted full civil rights in September 1791 and religious equality in 1795. The revolutionary and Napoleonic regimes abolished Jewish ghettoes in the territories they conquered.[[357]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer1984160–61-364) Napoleon wished to assimilate Jews into French society and convened an assembly of Jewish notables in 1806 to that end. In 1807, he summoned a [Sanhedrin](/wiki/Sanhedrin "Sanhedrin") to adapt the law of Moses to those of the empire. An imperial decree of March 1808 organized Jewish worship into consistories, limited usury and encouraged Jews to adopt a family name, intermarriage, and civil marriage and divorce.[[4]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200438–40-7)[[357]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer1984160–61-364) Jews, however, were still subject to discrimination in many parts of the empire and satellite states.[[356]](#cite_note-McLynn-1997a-363)

Personality
-----------

[Pieter Geyl](/wiki/Pieter_Geyl "Pieter Geyl") wrote in 1947, "It is impossible that two historians, especially two historians living in different periods, should see any historical personality in the same light."[[358]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeyl194915-365) There is no dispute that Napoleon was ambitious, although commentators disagree on whether his ambition was mostly for his own power and glory or for the welfare of France.[[359]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeyl1949135–37,_198-366)[[360]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECobban196318–19-367)[[361]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnett199788–89-368) Historians agree that Napoleon was highly intelligent with an excellent memory,[[362]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell201526-369)[[363]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECobban196318-370)[[364]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLynn1997280-83-371) and was a superior organizer who could work efficiently for long hours.[[363]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECobban196318-370)[[365]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLynn1997280–81-372) In battle, he could rapidly dictate a series of complex commands to his subordinates, keeping in mind where major units were expected to be at each future point.[[366]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChandler1966"Introduction",_pp._3-36-373)

He was an inspiring leader who could obtain the best from his soldiers and subordinates.[[367]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEnglund2010379ff-374) [Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington](/wiki/Arthur_Wellesley,_1st_Duke_of_Wellington "Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington") said his presence on the battlefield was worth 40,000 soldiers.[[368]](#cite_note-375)[[369]](#cite_note-376) He could charm people when he needed to but could also publicly humiliate them and was known for his rages when his plans were frustrated.[[370]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrice20148-377)[[371]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECobban196318-19-378)[[372]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLynn1997279–80-379)[[373]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeyl1949135–37-380) Historian McLynn sees him as a [misogynist](/wiki/Misogynist "Misogynist") with a cruel streak which he often inflicted on women, children and animals.[[374]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLynn1997277–79-381)

There is debate over whether Napoleon was an outsider who never felt at home in France or with other people.[[375]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLynn1997287-382) [Hippolyte Taine](/wiki/Hippolyte_Taine "Hippolyte Taine") said Napoleon saw others only as instruments and was cut off from feelings of admiration, sympathy or pity. Arthur Lévy replied that Napoleon genuinely loved Joséphine and often showed humanity and compassion to his enemies or those who had let him down. He had the normal middle class virtues and understood the common man.[[376]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeyl1949135–37,_175-383)

Similarly, historians are divided over whether Napoleon was consistently ruthless when his power was threatened or surprisingly indulgent in some cases. Those arguing for a ruthless personality point to episodes such as his violent suppression of revolts in France and conquered territories,[[377]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeyl1949198-384) his execution of the Duc d'Enghien and plotters against his rule,[[10]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell201552-13)[[378]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECobban196316–17-385) and his massacre of Turkish prisoners of war in Syria in 1799.[[372]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLynn1997279–80-379)[[104]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell201539–40-109) Others point to his mild treatment of disloyal subordinates such as [Charles XIV John](/wiki/Charles_XIV_John "Charles XIV John"), Talleyrand and Fouché.[[379]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLynn1997279-80-386)

Many historians see Napoleon as pragmatic and a realist, at least in the early years of his rule.[[380]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECobban196312-387)[[381]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLynn1997286-388)[[382]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2015a573-389) He was not driven by ideology and promoted capable men irrespective of their political and social background, as long as they were loyal.[[383]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECobban196321-390)[[384]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2015a573,_575–76-391) As an expert in military matters, he valued technical expertise and listened to the advice of experts in other fields.[[383]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECobban196321-390) However, there is a consensus that once he dominated Europe he became more intolerant of other views and surrounded himself with "yes men".[[385]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECobban196356-392)[[386]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2015a582-393) Towards the end of his reign he lost his realism and ability to compromise.[[387]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECobban196319,_47-394)[[388]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200495–96-395)

Some historians talk of Napoleon's dual nature: a rationalist with a strong romantic streak.[[389]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeyl194920-396)[[390]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLynn1997287–91-397) He took a team of scholars, artists and engineers with him to Egypt in order to scientifically study the country's culture and history, but at the same time was struck by romantic "orientalism". "I was full of dreams," he stated. "I saw myself founding a religion, marching into Asia, riding an elephant, a turban on my head and in my hand a new Koran that I would have composed to suit my need."[[391]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell201537-38-398)

Napoleon was superstitious. He believed in [omens](/wiki/Omen "Omen"), numerology, fate and lucky stars, and always asked of his generals: is he lucky?[[392]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLynn1997288–89-399) Dwyer states that Napoleon's victories at Austerlitz and Jena in 1805–06 left him even more certain of his destiny and invincibility.[[393]](#cite_note-400) "I am of the race that founds empires", he once boasted, deeming himself an heir to the Ancient Romans.[[394]](#cite_note-401)

Various psychologists have attempted to explain Napoleon's personality. [Alfred Adler](/wiki/Alfred_Adler "Alfred Adler") cited Napoleon to describe an [inferiority complex](/wiki/Inferiority_complex "Inferiority complex") in which short people adopt over-aggressive behaviour to compensate for lack of height; this inspired the term *[Napoleon complex](/wiki/Napoleon_complex "Napoleon complex")*.[[395]](#cite_note-402) Adler, [Erich Fromm](/wiki/Erich_Fromm "Erich Fromm") and [Wilhelm Reich](/wiki/Wilhelm_Reich "Wilhelm Reich") ascribed his nervous energy to [sexual dysfunction](/wiki/Sexual_dysfunction "Sexual dysfunction").[[396]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLynn1997285-403) Harold T. Parker speculated that rivalry with his older brother and bullying when he moved to France led him to develop an inferiority complex which made him domineering.[[397]](#cite_note-404)

Appearance and image
--------------------

Further information: [Cultural depictions of Napoleon](/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_Napoleon "Cultural depictions of Napoleon") and [Uniforms of Napoleon](/wiki/Uniforms_of_Napoleon "Uniforms of Napoleon")

In his youth, Bonaparte was consistently described as small and thin. Johann Ludwig Wurstemberger, who accompanied him in 1797 and 1798, noted that "Bonaparte was rather slight and emaciated-looking; his face, too, was very thin, with a dark complexion... his black, unpowdered hair hung down evenly over both shoulders", but that, despite his slight and unkempt appearance, "his looks and expression were earnest and powerful."[[398]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKircheisen1932129-405)

English painter [Joseph Farington](/wiki/Joseph_Farington "Joseph Farington"), who met him in 1802, said Bonaparte's eyes were "lighter, and more of a grey, than I should have expected from his complexion", that "his person is below middle size", and that "his general aspect was milder than I had before thought it."[[399]](#cite_note-406)

In his later years Napoleon gained weight and had a sallow complexion. Novelist Paul de Kock, who saw him in 1811, called Napoleon "yellow, obese, and bloated".[[400]](#cite_note-407)

During the [Napoleonic Wars](/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars "Napoleonic Wars"), the British press depicted Napoleon as a dangerous [tyrant](/wiki/Tyrant "Tyrant"), poised to invade. A nursery rhyme warned children that he ate naughty people; the "[bogeyman](/wiki/Bogeyman "Bogeyman")".[[401]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts200493-408) He was mocked as a short-tempered small man and was nicknamed "Little Boney in a strong fit".[[402]](#cite_note-nationalpostBoney2-409) In fact, at about 170 cm (5 ft 7 in), he was of average height.[[403]](#cite_note-410)[[404]](#cite_note-411)

He is often portrayed wearing a large [bicorne](/wiki/Bicorne "Bicorne") hat—sideways—with a [hand-in-waistcoat](/wiki/Hand-in-waistcoat "Hand-in-waistcoat") gesture—a reference to the painting produced in 1812 by [Jacques-Louis David](/wiki/Jacques-Louis_David "Jacques-Louis David").[[405]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBordes2007118-412)

Reforms
-------

Napoleon instituted numerous reforms, many of which had a lasting influence on France, Europe and the world. He reformed the French administration, codified French law, implemented a new education system, and established the first French central bank, the [Banque de France](/wiki/Bank_of_France "Bank of France").[[406]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell201553–56-413) He negotiated the [Concordat of 1801](/wiki/Concordat_of_1801 "Concordat of 1801") with the Catholic Church, which sought to reconcile the majority Catholic population to his regime. It was presented alongside the [Organic Articles](/wiki/Organic_Articles "Organic Articles"), which regulated public worship in France. He also implemented civil and religious equality for Protestants and Jews.[[407]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200437–40-414) In May 1802, he instituted the [Legion of Honour](/wiki/Legion_of_Honour "Legion of Honour") to encourage civilian and military achievements. The order is still the highest decoration in France.[[408]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlaufarb2008101–10-415)[[409]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200449–51-416) He introduced three French constitutions culminating in the reintroduction of a hereditary monarchy and nobility.[[410]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200429-35,_51-53-417)

### Administration

Napoleon introduced a series of centralizing administrative reforms soon after taking power. In 1800, he established prefects appointed to run France's regional departments, sub-prefects to run districts and mayors to run towns. Local representative bodies were retained, but their powers were reduced and indirect elections with a high property qualification replaced direct elections.[[411]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200475–76-418) Real power in the regions was now in the hands of the prefects who were judged by how they met the main priorities of Napoleon's government: efficient administration, law and order, stimulating the local economy, gathering votes for plebiscites, conscripting soldiers and provisioning the army.[[412]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECobban196324–25-419)[[413]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200476-420)

An enduring reform was the foundation, in December 1799, of the [Council of State](/wiki/Conseil_d%27%C3%89tat_(France) "Conseil d'État (France)"), an advisory body of experts which could also draft laws for submission to the legislative body. Napoleon drew many of his ministers and ambassadors from the council. It was the council which undertook the codification of French law.[[414]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECobban196321–23-421)

After several attempts by revolutionary governments, Napoleon officially introduced the [metric system](/wiki/Metric_system "Metric system") in France in 1801 and it was spread through western Europe by his armies.[[415]](#cite_note-422)[[416]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'ConnorRobertson2003-423) The new system was unpopular in some circles, so in 1812 he introduced a compromise system in the retail trade called the *[mesures usuelles](/wiki/Mesures_usuelles "Mesures usuelles")* (traditional units of measurement).[[417]](#cite_note-H&H2-424) In December 1805, Napoleon abolished the Revolutionary calendar, with its ten-day week, which had been introduced in 1793.[[418]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer1984234-425)

### Napoleonic Code

Main article: [Napoleonic Code](/wiki/Napoleonic_Code "Napoleonic Code")

Napoleon's [civil code of laws](/wiki/Civil_code "Civil code"), known from 1807 as the Napoleonic Code, was implemented in March 1804. It was prepared by committees of legal experts under the supervision of [Jean Jacques Régis de Cambacérès](/wiki/Jean-Jacques-R%C3%A9gis_de_Cambac%C3%A9r%C3%A8s "Jean-Jacques-Régis de Cambacérès"), the *Second Consul*. Napoleon participated actively in the sessions of the [Council of State](/wiki/Council_of_State_(France) "Council of State (France)") that revised the drafts. The code introduced a clearly written and accessible set of national laws to replace the various regional and customary law systems that had operated in France.[[419]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200441-426)

The civil code entrenched the principles of equality before the law, religious toleration, secure property rights, equal inheritance for all legitimate children, and the abolition of the vestiges of feudalism. However, it also reduced the rights of women and children and severely restricted the grounds for divorce.[[420]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECobban196327-28-427)[[421]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2015a577-78-428)

A criminal code was promulgated in 1808, and eventually seven codes of law were produced under Napoleon.[[422]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200443–44-429) The Napoleonic code was carried by Napoleon's armies across Europe and influenced the law in many parts of the world. [Alfred Cobban](/wiki/Alfred_Cobban "Alfred Cobban") described it as, "the most effective agency for the propagation of the basic principles of the French Revolution."[[423]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECobban196328-430)

### Warfare

Further information: [Napoleonic weaponry and warfare](/wiki/Napoleonic_weaponry_and_warfare "Napoleonic weaponry and warfare") and [Military career of Napoleon](/wiki/Military_career_of_Napoleon "Military career of Napoleon")

In the field of [military organization](/wiki/Military_organization "Military organization"), Napoleon borrowed from previous theorists such as [Jacques Antoine Hippolyte, Comte de Guibert](/wiki/Jacques_Antoine_Hippolyte,_Comte_de_Guibert "Jacques Antoine Hippolyte, Comte de Guibert"), and from the reforms of preceding French governments, and then developed what was already in place. He continued the Revolutionary policies of conscription and promotion based primarily on merit.[[424]](#cite_note-Archer-431)[[425]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlynn200116-432)

[Corps](/wiki/Corps "Corps") replaced divisions as the largest army units, [mobile artillery](/wiki/Self-propelled_artillery "Self-propelled artillery") was integrated into reserve batteries, the staff system became more fluid, and cavalry returned as an important formation in French military doctrine. These methods are now referred to as essential features of Napoleonic warfare.[[424]](#cite_note-Archer-431)

Napoleon was regarded by the influential military theorist [Carl von Clausewitz](/wiki/Carl_von_Clausewitz "Carl von Clausewitz") as a genius in the art of war, and many historians rank him as a great military commander.[[424]](#cite_note-Archer-431) Wellington considered him the greatest military commander of all time,[[426]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts2004272-433) and [Henry Vassall-Fox](/wiki/Henry_Vassall-Fox,_3rd_Baron_Holland "Henry Vassall-Fox, 3rd Baron Holland") called him "the greatest statesman and the ablest general of ancient or modern times".[[427]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts200159-434) Cobban states that he showed his genius in moving troops quickly and concentrating them on strategic points.[[428]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECobban196346–47-435) His principles were to keep his forces united, keep no weak point unguarded, seize important points quickly, and seize his chance.[[429]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200490-436) Owen Connelly, however, states, "Napoleon's personal tactics defy analysis." He used his intuition, engaged his troops, and reacted to what developed.[[430]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200493–94-437)

Napoleon was an aggressive commander with a preference for the offensive.[[431]](#cite_note-438) Under Napoleon, the focus shifted towards destroying enemy armies rather than simply outmanoeuvering them. Wars became more costly and decisive as invasions of enemy territory occurred on larger fronts. The political cost of war also increased, as defeat for a European power now meant more than just losing isolated territories. Peace terms were often punitive, sometimes involving regime change, which intensified the trend towards [total war](/wiki/Total_war "Total war") since the Revolutionary era.[[424]](#cite_note-Archer-431)[[432]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell201510–13-439)

### Education

Napoleon's educational reforms laid the foundation of a modern system of secondary and tertiary education in France and throughout much of Europe.[[433]](#cite_note-440) He synthesized academic elements from the *[Ancien Régime](/wiki/Ancien_R%C3%A9gime "Ancien Régime")*, [The Enlightenment](/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment "Age of Enlightenment"), and [the Revolution](/wiki/French_Revolution "French Revolution").[[434]](#cite_note-441) His education laws of 1802 left most primary education in the hands of religious or communal schools which taught basic literacy and numeracy for a minority of the population.[[435]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECobban196334-442) He abolished the revolutionary central schools and replaced them with secondary schools and elite lycées where the curriculum was based on reading, writing, mathematics, Latin, natural history, classics, and ancient history.[[436]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200458–59-443)

He retained the revolutionary higher education system, with *grandes écoles* in professions including law, medicine, pharmacy, engineering and school teaching. He introduced *grandes écoles* in history and geography, but opposed one in literature because it was not vocational. He also founded the military academy of Saint Cyr.[[437]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200460-444) He promoted the advanced centres, such as the [École Polytechnique](/wiki/%C3%89cole_Polytechnique "École Polytechnique"), that provided both military expertise and advanced research in science.[[438]](#cite_note-445)

In 1808, he founded the Imperial University, a supervisory body with control over curriculum and discipline. The following year he introduced the baccalaureate.[[439]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200459-446) The system was designed to produce the efficient bureaucrats, technicians, professionals and military officers that the Napoleonic state required. It outperformed its European counterparts, many of which borrowed from the French system.[[440]](#cite_note-447)

Female education, in contrast, was designed to be practical and religious, based on home science, the catechism, basic literacy and numeracy, and enough science to eradicate superstition.[[441]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200460–61-448)

### Nobility and honours

In May 1802, Bonaparte created the Legion of Honour whose members would be military personnel and civilians with distinguished service to the state. The new institution was unpopular with republicans and the measure only passed by 14 votes to 10 in the Council of State.[[442]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200449-449) The Legion of Honour became an order of chivalry after the empire was proclaimed in 1804. In August 1806, Napoleon created an hereditary imperial nobility including princes, dukes, counts, barons and knights. Eventually the empire had over 3,000 nobles and more than 30,000 members of the Legion of Honour.[[443]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZamoyski2018358,_398–99-450)[[444]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200450–51-451)

Memory and evaluation
---------------------

Main article: [Legacy of Napoleon](/wiki/Legacy_of_Napoleon "Legacy of Napoleon")

### Criticism

There is debate over whether Napoleon was "an [enlightened despot](/wiki/Enlightened_absolutism "Enlightened absolutism") who laid the foundations of modern Europe" or "a [megalomaniac](/wiki/Narcissistic_personality_disorder "Narcissistic personality disorder") who wrought greater misery than any man before the coming of Hitler".[[445]](#cite_note-452) He was compared to [Adolf Hitler](/wiki/Adolf_Hitler "Adolf Hitler") by [Pieter Geyl](/wiki/Pieter_Geyl "Pieter Geyl") in 1947,[[446]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeyl19497–10-453) and [Claude Ribbe](/wiki/Claude_Ribbe "Claude Ribbe") in 2005.[[447]](#cite_note-454) Most modern critics of Napoleon, however, reject the Hitler comparison, arguing that Napoleon did not commit genocide and did not engage in the mass murder and imprisonment of his political opponents.[[448]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLynn1997666–67-455)[[449]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChandler1973xliii-456) Nevertheless, [David A. Bell](/wiki/David_A._Bell "David A. Bell") and McLynn condemn his killing of 3,000–5,000 Turkish prisoners of war in Syria.[[104]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell201539–40-109)[[105]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLynn1997280-110)

A number of historians have argued that his expansionist foreign policy was a major factor in the Napoleonic wars,[[450]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2015a574-457)[[451]](#cite_note-458) which cost six million lives and caused economic disruption for a generation.[[452]](#cite_note-459)[[453]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLynn1997666-460) McLynn and [Correlli Barnett](/wiki/Correlli_Barnett "Correlli Barnett") suggest that Napoleon's reputation as a military genius is exaggerated.[[454]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnett199741,_53,_75,_103-461)[[455]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLynn1997665-462) Cobban and Susan P. Conner argue that Napoleon had insufficient regard for the lives of his soldiers and that his battle tactics led to excessive casualties.[[456]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECobban196319-463)[[457]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200462,_105–07-464)

Critics also cite Napoleon's exploitation of conquered territories.[[455]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLynn1997665-462) To finance his wars, Napoleon increased taxes and levies of troops from annexed territories and satellite states.[[458]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200481–82-465)[[459]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECobban196329,_46-466) He also introduced discriminatory tariff policies which promoted French trade at the expense of allies and satellite states.[[460]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECobban196352-467) He institutionalized plunder: French museums contain art stolen by Napoleon's forces from across Europe. Artefacts were brought to the [Musée du Louvre](/wiki/Louvre "Louvre") for a grand central museum; an example which would later be followed by others.[[461]](#cite_note-468)

Many historians have criticized Napoleon's authoritarian rule, especially after 1807, which included censorship, the closure of independent newspapers, the bypassing of direct elections and representative government, the dismissal of judges showing independence, and the exile of critics of the regime.[[8]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2015a574–76,_582–84-11)[[462]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200432–34-469)[[10]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell201552-13) Historians also blame Napoleon for reducing the civil rights of women, children and people of colour, and reintroducing the legal penalties of [civil death](/wiki/Civil_death "Civil death") and confiscation of property.[[463]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2015a578,_584-470)[[462]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200432–34-469)[[420]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECobban196327-28-427) His reintroduction of an hereditary monarchy and nobility remains controversial.[[442]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner200449-449)[[464]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2015a579–84-471) His role in the [Haitian Revolution](/wiki/Haitian_Revolution "Haitian Revolution") and decision to reinstate slavery in France's colonies in the Caribbean and Indian Ocean adversely affect his reputation.[[465]](#cite_note-Repa22-472)[[466]](#cite_note-473)

### Propaganda and memory

Main articles: [Napoleonic propaganda](/wiki/Napoleonic_propaganda "Napoleonic propaganda") and [Bonapartism](/wiki/Bonapartism "Bonapartism")

Napoleon's use of propaganda contributed to his rise to power, legitimated his regime, and established his image for posterity. Strict censorship and control of the [press](/wiki/Mass_media "Mass media"), books, theatre, and art were part of his propaganda scheme, aimed at portraying him as bringing peace and stability to France. Propaganda focused on his role first as a general then as a civil leader and emperor. He fostered a relationship with artists, commissioning and controlling different forms of art to suit his propaganda goals.[[467]](#cite_note-474)

Napoleonic propaganda survived his exile to Saint Helena. Las Cases, who was with Napoleon in exile, published *[The Memorial of Saint Helena](/wiki/The_Memorial_of_Saint_Helena "The Memorial of Saint Helena")* in 1822, creating a legend of Napoleon as a liberal, visionary proponent of European unification, deposed by reactionary elements of the *Ancien Régime*.[[468]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrice2014262-475)[[469]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell2015106-476) Napoleon remained a central figure in the romantic art and literature of the 1820s and 1830s.[[470]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell2015107-477)

The Napoleonic legend played a key role in collective political defiance of the Bourbon restoration monarchy in 1815–1830. People from different walks of life and areas of France, particularly Napoleonic veterans, drew on the Napoleonic legacy and its connections with the ideals of the 1789 Revolution.[[471]](#cite_note-Memory-478) The defiance manifested itself in seditious materials, displaying the tricolour and rosettes. There were also subversive activities celebrating anniversaries of Napoleon's life and reign and disrupting royal celebrations.[[471]](#cite_note-Memory-478)

Bell sees the return of Napoleon's remains to France in 1840 as an attempt by Louis-Phillipe to prop up his unpopular regime by associating it with Napoleon, and that the regime of Napoleon III was only possible due to the continued resonance of the Napoleonic legend.[[472]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell2015107–109-479)

Venita Datta argues that following the collapse of militaristic [Boulangism](/wiki/Georges_Ernest_Boulanger "Georges Ernest Boulanger") in the late 1880s, the Napoleonic legend was divorced from party politics and revived in popular culture. Writers and critics of the *[Belle Époque](/wiki/Belle_%C3%89poque "Belle Époque")* exploited the Napoleonic legend for diverse political and cultural ends.[[473]](#cite_note-480)

In the 21st century, Napoleon appears regularly in popular fiction, drama and advertising. Napoleon and his era remain major topics of historical research with a sharp increase in historical books, articles and symposia during the bicentenary years of 1999 to 2015.[[474]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell2015109–12-481)[[475]](#cite_note-482)

* *[Napoleon Crossing the Alps](/wiki/Napoleon_Crossing_the_Alps "Napoleon Crossing the Alps")*, [romantic](/wiki/Romanticism "Romanticism") version by [Jacques-Louis David](/wiki/Jacques-Louis_David "Jacques-Louis David") in 1805
* *[Bonaparte Crossing the Alps](/wiki/Bonaparte_Crossing_the_Alps "Bonaparte Crossing the Alps")*, [realist](/wiki/Realism_(art_movement) "Realism (art movement)") version by [Paul Delaroche](/wiki/Paul_Delaroche "Paul Delaroche") in 1848
* Moscow (1812). *Napoleon leaves the Kremlin*, part of the [French occupation of Moscow](/wiki/French_occupation_of_Moscow "French occupation of Moscow"), painting by [Maurice Orange](/wiki/Maurice_Orange "Maurice Orange").

### Long-term influence outside France

Main article: [Influence of the French Revolution](/wiki/Influence_of_the_French_Revolution "Influence of the French Revolution")

Napoleon was responsible for spreading many of the values of the French Revolution to other countries, especially through the Napoleonic Code.[[476]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrab20172016ff-483) After the fall of Napoleon, it continued to influence the law in western Europe and other parts of the world including Latin America, the Dominican Republic, Louisiana and Quebec.[[477]](#cite_note-484)

Napoleon's regime abolished remnants of feudalism in the lands he conquered and in his satellite states. He liberalized [property laws](/wiki/Property_law "Property law"), ended [manorialism](/wiki/Manorialism "Manorialism"), abolished the [guild](/wiki/Guild "Guild") of merchants and craftsmen to facilitate entrepreneurship, legalized divorce, closed the Jewish [ghettos](/wiki/Ghetto "Ghetto") and ended the [Spanish Inquisition](/wiki/Spanish_Inquisition "Spanish Inquisition"). The power of church courts and religious authority was sharply reduced and [equality before the law](/wiki/Equality_before_the_law "Equality before the law") was proclaimed for all men.[[478]](#cite_note-485)

Napoleon reorganized what had been the [Holy Roman Empire](/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire "Holy Roman Empire"), made up of about three hundred *[Kleinstaaten](/w/index.php?title=Kleinstaaten&action=edit&redlink=1 "Kleinstaaten (page does not exist)")*, into a more streamlined forty-state [Confederation of the Rhine](/wiki/Confederation_of_the_Rhine "Confederation of the Rhine"); this helped promote the [German Confederation](/wiki/German_Confederation "German Confederation") and the [unification of Germany](/wiki/Unification_of_Germany "Unification of Germany") in 1871, as it sparked a new wave of [German nationalism](/wiki/German_nationalism "German nationalism") that opposed the French intervention.[[479]](#cite_note-486)

The movement toward [Italian unification](/wiki/Italian_unification "Italian unification") was similarly sparked by Napoleonic rule.[[480]](#cite_note-487) These changes contributed to the development of nationalism and the [nation state](/wiki/Nation_state "Nation state").[[481]](#cite_note-488)

The Napoleonic invasion of Spain and ousting of the Spanish Bourbon monarchy had a significant effect on [Spanish America](/wiki/Hispanic_America "Hispanic America"). Many local elites sought to rule in the name of [Ferdinand VII of Spain](/wiki/Ferdinand_VII_of_Spain "Ferdinand VII of Spain"), whom they considered the legitimate monarch. Napoleon indirectly began the process of [Latin American independence](/wiki/Spanish_American_wars_of_independence "Spanish American wars of independence") when the power vacuum was filled by local political leaders such as [Simón Bolívar](/wiki/Sim%C3%B3n_Bol%C3%ADvar "Simón Bolívar") and [José de San Martín](/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_de_San_Mart%C3%ADn "José de San Martín"). Such leaders embraced nationalistic sentiments influenced by French nationalism and led successful independence movements in Latin America.[[482]](#cite_note-489)[[483]](#cite_note-490)

Napoleon's reputation is generally favourable in Poland, which is the only country in the world to evoke him in its national anthem, [Poland Is Not Yet Lost](/wiki/Poland_Is_Not_Yet_Lost "Poland Is Not Yet Lost").[[484]](#cite_note-491)

Children
--------

Napoleon married Joséphine in 1796, but the marriage produced no children.[[485]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013320–21-492) In 1806, he adopted his step-son, [Eugène de Beauharnais](/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_de_Beauharnais "Eugène de Beauharnais") (1781–1824), and his second cousin, [Stéphanie de Beauharnais](/wiki/St%C3%A9phanie_de_Beauharnais "Stéphanie de Beauharnais") (1789–1860), and arranged dynastic marriages for them.[[486]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLynn1997318-19-493)

Napoleon's marriage to Marie Louise produced one child, Napoleon Francis Joseph Charles ([Napoleon II](/wiki/Napoleon_II "Napoleon II")) (1811–1832), known from birth as the [King of Rome](/wiki/King_of_the_Romans "King of the Romans"). When Napoleon abdicated in 1815 he named his son his successor as "Napoleon II", but the allies refused to recognize him. He was awarded the title of the Duke of Reichstadt in 1818 and died of [tuberculosis](/wiki/Tuberculosis "Tuberculosis") aged 21, with no children.[[487]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer1984203-494)[[488]](#cite_note-McLynn_1997,_p.6632-495)

Napoleon acknowledged one illegitimate son: [Charles Léon](/wiki/Charles_L%C3%A9on "Charles Léon") (1806–1881) by [Eléonore Denuelle de La Plaigne](/wiki/El%C3%A9onore_Denuelle_de_La_Plaigne "Eléonore Denuelle de La Plaigne").[[489]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer1984105-496)[[490]](#cite_note-m6302-497) [Alexandre Colonna-Walewski](/wiki/Alexandre_Colonna-Walewski "Alexandre Colonna-Walewski") (1810–1868), the son of his Polish mistress [Maria Walewska](/wiki/Maria_Walewska "Maria Walewska"), was also widely known to be his child,[[485]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer2013320–21-492) as DNA evidence has confirmed.[[491]](#cite_note-498) He may have had further illegitimate offspring.[[492]](#cite_note-McLynn4232-499)

Arms
----

See also: [Armorial of the First French Empire](/wiki/Armorial_of_the_First_French_Empire "Armorial of the First French Empire")

On becoming emperor, Napoleon adopted the [French Imperial Eagle](/wiki/French_Imperial_Eagle "French Imperial Eagle") as his arms.[[493]](#cite_note-500)

Imperial Arms of Napoleon

Arms: *Azure, an Eagle Or, head facing to the sinister, clutching in its talons a Thunderbolt Or.*

Achievement of Napoleon

Notes
-----

1. ^ [***a***](#cite_ref-louis_reign_1-0) [***b***](#cite_ref-louis_reign_1-1) As [King of France](/wiki/King_of_France "King of France")
2. **[^](#cite_ref-2)** English: [/nəˈpoʊliən ˈboʊnəpɑːrt/](/wiki/Help:IPA/English "Help:IPA/English") [*nə-POH-lee-ən BOH-nə-part*](/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key "Help:Pronunciation respelling key"); French: *Napoléon Bonaparte* [[napɔleɔ̃ bɔnapaʁt]](/wiki/Help:IPA/French "Help:IPA/French").
3. **[^](#cite_ref-4)** Italian: [[napoleˈoːne ˌbwɔnaˈparte]](/wiki/Help:IPA/Italian "Help:IPA/Italian"); [Corsican](/wiki/Corsican_language "Corsican language"): *Napulione Buonaparte* [[napuliˈɔnɛ ˌbwɔnaˈbartɛ]](/wiki/Help:IPA/Corsican "Help:IPA/Corsican").
4. **[^](#cite_ref-23)** Although the [1768 Treaty of Versailles](/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles_(1768) "Treaty of Versailles (1768)") formally ceded Corsica's rights, it remained un-incorporated during 1769[[16]](#cite_note-McLynn-1997b-19) until it became one of the [Provinces of France](/wiki/Provinces_of_France "Provinces of France") in 1770.[[17]](#cite_note-20) Corsica would be legally integrated as a [département](/wiki/Departments_of_France "Departments of France") in 1789.[[18]](#cite_note-21)[[19]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts2014142-22)
5. **[^](#cite_ref-35)** Aside from his name, there does not appear to be a connection between him and [Napoleon's theorem](/wiki/Napoleon%27s_theorem "Napoleon's theorem").[[30]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWells199274-34)
6. **[^](#cite_ref-133)** This is depicted in *[Bonaparte Crossing the Alps](/wiki/Bonaparte_Crossing_the_Alps "Bonaparte Crossing the Alps")* by [Hippolyte Delaroche](/wiki/Hippolyte_Delaroche "Hippolyte Delaroche") and in Jacques-Louis David's imperial *Napoleon Crossing the Alps*. He is less realistically portrayed on a [charger](/wiki/Horses_in_warfare "Horses in warfare") in the latter work.[[127]](#cite_note-132)
7. **[^](#cite_ref-284)** There were actually three versions of the act written on 4 April 1814. The final signed version explicitly refers to "Napoleon II" as his successor.[[277]](#cite_note-283)

Paris. \*The Arms depicts a shield with a golden eagle in front of a blue background, within its talons clutching a thunderbolt. The shield is surrounded by Napoleon's red Imperial mantle, filled with golden bees. The shield is topped by the Imperial crown, which sits atop a golden Imperial helmet. Surrounding the shield is the chain and pendant of the Legion d'honneur. Crossed behind the shield are the Scepters of justice and mercy.

* Schroeder, Paul W. (1996). [*The Transformation of European Politics 1763–1848*](https://books.google.com/books?id=BS2z3iGPCigC). Oxford U.P. pp. 177–560. [ISBN](/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-0-1982-0654-5](/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-1982-0654-5 "Special:BookSources/978-0-1982-0654-5"). Retrieved 1 July 2015. advanced diplomatic history of Napoleon and his era
